	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Chinese characters</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hanzi&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanzi">Hanzi</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Unless otherwise specified, Chinese text in this article is written in the format <i><a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified Chinese">Simplified Chinese</a> / <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional Chinese">Traditional Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">Pinyin</a></i>. If the Simplified and Traditional Chinese characters are identical, they are written only once.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the moth known as the "Chinese character", see <a href="/wiki/Cilix_glaucata" title="Cilix glaucata"><i>Cilix glaucata</i></a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">logographic writing system used in the Sinosphere region</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;border-top:1px solid #c9b6a3;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Chinese_characters_sidebar" title="Template:Chinese characters sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Chinese_characters_sidebar" title="Template talk:Chinese characters sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Chinese_characters_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Chinese characters</b> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hans"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B1%89%E5%AD%97" class="extiw" title="wikt:汉字">汉字</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hant"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%BC%A2%E5%AD%97" class="extiw" title="wikt:漢字">漢字</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/h%C3%A0nz%C3%AC" class="extiw" title="wikt:hànzì">hànzì</a></span></i>; literally&#58; 'Han characters') are <a href="/wiki/Logogram" title="Logogram">logograms</a> developed for the <a href="/wiki/Written_Chinese" title="Written Chinese">writing of Chinese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>  They have been adapted to write a number of other Asian languages. They remain a key component of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_writing_system" title="Japanese writing system">Japanese writing system</a> where they are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a></i>. Chinese characters constitute the oldest continuously used system of writing in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> By virtue of their widespread current use in East Asia, and historic use throughout the <a href="/wiki/Adoption_of_Chinese_literary_culture" title="Adoption of Chinese literary culture">Sinosphere</a>, Chinese characters are among the most widely adopted writing systems in the world by number of users. Chinese characters number in the tens of thousands, though most of them are minor graphic variants encountered only in historical texts. Unlike an alphabet, a character-based writing system associates each logogram with an entire sound and thus may be compared in some aspects to a syllabary.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Functional_literacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Functional literacy">Functional literacy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Written_Chinese" title="Written Chinese">written Chinese</a> requires a knowledge of between three and four thousand characters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198873_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198873-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In Japan, 2,136 are taught through secondary school (the <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">Jōyō kanji</a></i>); hundreds more are in everyday use. Due to <a href="/wiki/Shinjitai" title="Shinjitai">post-WWII simplifications of characters in Japan</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">in China</a>, the Chinese characters used in Japan today are distinct from those used in China in several respects. There are various national standard lists of characters, forms, and pronunciations. <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Simplified forms of certain characters</a> are used in mainland China, Singapore, and Malaysia; the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional characters</a> are used in Taiwan, Hong Kong, Macau, and to a limited extent in South Korea. In Japan, common characters are written in <a href="/wiki/Shinjitai" title="Shinjitai">post-WWII Japan-specific simplified forms</a>, while uncommon characters are written in <a href="/wiki/Ky%C5%ABjitai" title="Kyūjitai">Japanese traditional forms</a>, which are virtually identical to Chinese traditional forms.
</p><p>In modern Chinese, the majority of Chinese words today consist of two or more characters.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> A character almost always corresponds to a single syllable that is also a <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> However, there are a few exceptions to this general correspondence, including bisyllabic morphemes (written with two characters), bimorphemic syllables (written with two characters) and cases where a single character represents a polysyllabic word or phrase.<sup id="cite_ref-vmair_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vmair-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern Chinese has many <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">homophones</a>; thus the same spoken syllable may be represented by many characters, depending on meaning. A single character may also have a range of meanings, or sometimes quite distinct meanings; occasionally these correspond to different pronunciations. <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">Cognates</a> in the several <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a> are generally written with the same character. In other languages, most significantly today in Japanese and sometimes in Korean, characters are used to represent Chinese loanwords, to represent native words independently of the Chinese pronunciation (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Kunyomi" class="mw-redirect" title="Kunyomi">kunyomi</a> in Japanese), and as purely phonetic elements based on their pronunciation in the historical variety of Chinese from which they were acquired. These foreign adaptations of Chinese pronunciation are known as <a href="/wiki/Sino-Xenic_pronunciations" title="Sino-Xenic pronunciations">Sino-Xenic pronunciations</a> and have been useful in the <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_reconstruction" title="Linguistic reconstruction">reconstruction</a> of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Dominance_and_maturation_of_regular_script"><span class="tocnumber">3.8</span> <span class="toctext">Dominance and maturation of regular script</span></a></li>

</ul>
</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Function">Function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When the script was first used in the late 2nd millennium&#160;BC, words of <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> were generally monosyllabic, and each character denoted a single word.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198858_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198858-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Increasing numbers of polysyllabic words have entered the language from the <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou</a> period to the present day. It is estimated that about 25–30% of the vocabulary of classic texts from the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> was polysyllabic, though these words were used far less commonly than monosyllables, which accounted for 80–90% of occurrences in these texts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson201222_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson201222-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
The process has accelerated over the centuries as phonetic change has increased the number of homophones.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988112_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988112-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
It has been estimated that over two thirds of the 3,000 most common words in modern <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> are polysyllables, the vast majority of those being disyllables.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYip200018_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYip200018-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common process has been to form <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compounds</a> of existing words, written with the characters of the constituent words. Words have also been created by adding <a href="/wiki/Affixes" class="mw-redirect" title="Affixes">affixes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reduplication" title="Reduplication">reduplication</a> and <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">borrowing</a> from other languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988155–156_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988155–156-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
Polysyllabic words are generally written with one character per syllable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198874_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198874-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
In most cases the character denotes a <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a> descended from an Old Chinese word.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198874–75_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198874–75-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many characters have multiple readings, with instances denoting different morphemes, sometimes with different pronunciations. In modern Standard Chinese, one fifth of the 2,400 most common characters have multiple pronunciations.
For the 500 most common characters, the proportion rises to 30%.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
Often these readings are similar in sound and related in meaning. In the Old Chinese period, <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixes</a> could be added to a word to form a new word, which was often written with the same character. In many cases the pronunciations diverged due to subsequent <a href="/wiki/Sound_change" title="Sound change">sound change</a>. For example, many additional readings have the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> departing tone, the major source of the 4th tone in modern Standard Chinese. Scholars now believe that this tone is the reflex of an Old Chinese *-s suffix, with a range of semantic functions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315–317_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315–317-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
For example,
</p>
<ul><li><span lang="zh-Hans">传</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">傳</span> has readings OC *drjon &gt; MC <i>drjwen</i> &gt; Mod. <i>chuán</i> 'to transmit' and *drjons &gt; <i>drjwenH</i> &gt; <i>zhuàn</i> 'a record'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> (Middle Chinese forms are given in <a href="/wiki/Baxter%27s_transcription_for_Middle_Chinese" title="Baxter&#39;s transcription for Middle Chinese">Baxter's transcription</a>, in which <i>H</i> denotes the departing tone.)</li>
<li><span lang="zh">磨</span> has readings *maj &gt; <i>ma</i> &gt; <i>mó</i> 'to grind' and *majs &gt; <i>maH</i> &gt; <i>mò</i> 'grindstone'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span lang="zh">宿</span> has readings *sjuk &gt; <i>sjuwk</i> &gt; <i>sù</i> 'to stay overnight' and *sjuks &gt; <i>sjuwH</i> &gt; <i>xiù</i> 'celestial "mansion"'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992316_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992316-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span lang="zh-Hans">说</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">説</span> has readings *hljot &gt; <i>sywet</i> &gt; <i>shuō</i> 'speak' and *hljots &gt; <i>sywejH</i> &gt; <i>shuì</i> 'exhort'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992197,_305_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992197,_305-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Another common alternation is between voiced and voiceless initials (though the voicing distinction has disappeared on most modern varieties).
This is believed to reflect an ancient prefix, but scholars disagree on whether the voiced or voiceless form is the original root.
For example,
</p>
<ul><li><span lang="zh-Hans">见</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">見</span> has readings *kens &gt; <i>kenH</i> &gt; <i>jiàn</i> 'to see' and *gens &gt; <i>henH</i> &gt; <i>xiàn</i> 'to appear'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992218_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992218-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span lang="zh-Hans">败</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">敗</span> has readings *prats &gt; <i>pæjH</i> &gt; <i>bài</i> 'to defeat' and *brats &gt; <i>bæjH</i> &gt; <i>bài</i> 'to be defeated'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992218_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992218-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> (In this case the pronunciations have converged in Standard Chinese, but not in some other varieties.)</li>
<li><span lang="zh">折</span> has readings *tjat &gt; <i>tsyet</i> &gt; <i>zhé</i> 'to bend' and *djat &gt; <i>dzyet</i> &gt; <i>shé</i> 'to break by bending'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992219_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992219-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Principles_of_formation">Principles of formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Principles of formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chineseprimer3.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Chineseprimer3.png/150px-Chineseprimer3.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/Chineseprimer3.png 1.5x" data-file-width="225" data-file-height="338" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chineseprimer3.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Excerpt from a 1436 primer on Chinese characters</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_character_classification" title="Chinese character classification">Chinese character classification</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption-center{text-align:center;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Evo-r%C3%AC.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Evo-r%C3%AC.svg/300px-Evo-r%C3%AC.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="56" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Evo-r%C3%AC.svg/450px-Evo-r%C3%AC.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Evo-r%C3%AC.svg/600px-Evo-r%C3%AC.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="150" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Sun</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Evo-sh%C4%81n.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Evo-sh%C4%81n.svg/300px-Evo-sh%C4%81n.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="90" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Evo-sh%C4%81n.svg/450px-Evo-sh%C4%81n.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Evo-sh%C4%81n.svg/600px-Evo-sh%C4%81n.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="150" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Mountain</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Evo-elephant.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Evo-elephant.png/300px-Evo-elephant.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="96" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Evo-elephant.png/450px-Evo-elephant.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Evo-elephant.png/600px-Evo-elephant.png 2x" data-file-width="998" data-file-height="321" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Elephant</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="background-color:transparent">Evolution of pictograms</div></div></div></div>
<p>Chinese characters represent words of the language using several strategies. A few characters, including some of the most commonly used, were originally <a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">pictograms</a>, which depicted the objects denoted, or <a href="/wiki/Ideogram" title="Ideogram">ideograms</a>, in which meaning was expressed <a href="/wiki/Iconicity" title="Iconicity">iconically</a>. The vast majority were written using the <a href="/wiki/Rebus_principle" class="mw-redirect" title="Rebus principle">rebus principle</a>, in which a character for a similarly sounding word was either simply borrowed or (more commonly) extended with a disambiguating semantic marker to form a phono-semantic compound character.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198858–61_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198858–61-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The traditional six-fold classification (<i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">liùshū</i></i> <span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">六书</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">六書</span> "six writings") was first described by the scholar <a href="/wiki/Xu_Shen" title="Xu Shen">Xu Shen</a> in the postface of his dictionary <i><a href="/wiki/Shuowen_Jiezi" title="Shuowen Jiezi">Shuowen Jiezi</a></i> in 100&#160;AD.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198867–69_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198867–69-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
While this analysis is sometimes problematic and arguably fails to reflect the complete nature of the Chinese writing system, it has been perpetuated by its long history and pervasive use.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pictograms">Pictograms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pictograms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">象形字</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">xiàngxíngzì</i></i></li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">Pictograms</a> are highly stylized and simplified pictures of material objects.  Examples of pictograms include <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">日</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">rì</i></i> for "sun", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">月</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">yuè</i></i> for "moon", and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">木</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">mù</i></i> for "tree" or "wood". Xu Shen placed approximately 4% of characters in this category. 
Though few in number and expressing literal objects, pictograms and ideograms are nonetheless the basis on which all the more complex characters such as associative idea characters  (会意字) and pictophonetic characters (形声字) are formed.
</p><p>Pictograms are primary characters in the sense that they, along with ideograms (indicative characters i.e. symbols), are the building blocks of compound characters (意意字) and picto-phonetic characters (形声字). 
</p><p>Over time pictograms were increasingly standardized, simplified, and stylized to make them easier to write. Furthermore, the same kangxi radical character element can be used to depict different objects. 
Thus, the image depicted by most pictograms is not often immediately evident. For example 口 may indicate the mouth, a window as in 高 which depicts a tall building as a symbol of the idea of "tall" or the lip of a vessel as in 富 a wine jar under a roof as symbol of wealth.  That is, pictograms extended from literal objects to take on symbolic or metaphoric meanings; sometimes even displacing the use of the character as a literal term, or creating ambiguity, which was resolved though character determinants, more commonly but less accurately known as "radicals" i.e. concept keys in the phono-semantic characters.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_ideograms">Simple ideograms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Simple ideograms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">指事字</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">zhǐshìzì</i></i></li></ul>
<p>Also called <i>simple indicatives,</i> this small category contains characters that are direct <a href="/wiki/Iconicity" title="Iconicity">iconic</a> illustrations. Examples include <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">上</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">shàng</i></i> "up" and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">下</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">xià</i></i> "down", originally a dot above and below a line. Indicative characters are symbols for abstract concepts which could not be depicted literally but nonetheless can be expressed as a visual symbol e.g. concave 凸 ， convex 凹, flat-and-level 平
</p>
<h3><span id="Associative_idea_characters_.28compound_conceptual_characters.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Associative_idea_characters_(compound_conceptual_characters)">Associative idea characters (compound conceptual characters)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Associative idea characters (compound conceptual characters)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">会意字</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">會意字</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">huìyìzì</i></i></li></ul>
<p>Also translated as logical aggregates or associative compounds, these characters have been interpreted as combining two or more pictographic or ideographic characters to suggest a third meaning.  The canonical example is <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">明</span> bright. 明 is the association of the two brightest objects in the sky the sun 日 and moon 月, brought together to express the idea of "bright". It is canonical because the term 明白 in Chinese (lit. "bright white") means "to understand, understand". Other commonly cited examples include <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">休</span> "rest" (composed of the pictograms <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">人</span> "person" and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">木</span> "tree") and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">好</span> "good" (composed of <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">女</span> "woman" and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">子</span> "child").
</p><p>Xu Shen placed approximately 13% of characters in this category, but many of his examples are now believed to be phono-semantic compounds whose origin has been obscured by subsequent changes in their form.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013261_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013261-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Boodberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Boodberg">Peter Boodberg</a> and William Boltz go so far as to deny that any of the compound characters devised in ancient times were of this type, maintaining that now-lost "secondary readings" are responsible for the apparent absence of phonetic indicators,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoltz1994104–110_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoltz1994104–110-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> but their arguments have been rejected by other scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013265–268_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013265–268-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast, associative compound characters are common among <a href="/wiki/Kokuji" class="mw-redirect" title="Kokuji">characters coined in Japan</a>. Also, a few characters coined in China in modern times, such as <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">鉑</span> platinum, "white metal" (see <a href="/wiki/Chemical_elements_in_East_Asian_languages" title="Chemical elements in East Asian languages">chemical elements in East Asian languages</a>) belong to this category.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebus">Rebus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Rebus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">假借字</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">jiǎjièzì</i></i></li></ul>
<p>Also called <i>borrowings</i> or <i>phonetic loan characters,</i> the <a href="/wiki/Rebus" title="Rebus">rebus</a> category covers cases where an existing character is used to represent an unrelated word with similar or identical pronunciation; sometimes the old meaning is then lost completely, as with characters such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">自</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">zì</i></i>, which has lost its original meaning of "nose" completely and exclusively means "oneself", or <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">萬</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">wàn</i></i>, which originally meant "scorpion" but is now used only in the sense of "ten thousand".
</p><p>Rebus was pivotal in the history of writing in China insofar as it represented the stage at which logographic writing could become purely phonetic (phonographic). Chinese characters used purely for their sound values are attested in the <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">Chun Qiu</i></i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">春秋</span> and <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">Zhan Guo</i></i> <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">戰國</span> manuscripts, in which <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">zhi</i></i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">氏</span> was used to write <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">shi</i></i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">是</span> and vice versa, just lines apart; the same happened with <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">shao</i></i> 勺 for <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">Zhao</i></i> <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">趙</span>, with the characters in question being homophonous or nearly homophonous at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoltz1994169_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoltz1994169-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonetical_usage_for_foreign_words">Phonetical usage for foreign words</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Phonetical usage for foreign words">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Chinese characters are used rebus-like and exclusively for their phonetic value when transcribing words of foreign origin, such as ancient Buddhist terms or modern foreign names. For example, the word for the country "<a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>" is 罗马尼亚 (Luó Mǎ Ní Yà), in which the Chinese characters are only used for their sounds and do not provide any meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-AEG_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEG-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This usage is similar to that of the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">Katakana</a>, although the Katakanas use a special set of simplified forms of Chinese characters, in order to advertize their value as purely phonetic symbols. The same rebus principle for names in particular has also been used in <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">Egyptian hieroglyphs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maya_hieroglyphs" class="mw-redirect" title="Maya hieroglyphs">Maya hieroglyphs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DW_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DW-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In the Chinese usage, in a few instances, the characters used for pronunciation might be carefully chosen in order to connote a specific meaning, as regularly happens for brand names: <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> is translated phonetically as 可口可乐 (Kěkǒu Kělè), but the characters were carefully selected so as to have the additional meaning of "Delicious and Enjoyable".<sup id="cite_ref-AEG_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEG-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DW_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DW-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phono-semantic_compounds">Phono-semantic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Phono-semantic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">形声字</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">形聲字</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">xíngshēngzì</i></i></li></ul>

style="width:32px;max-width:32px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Busyu-kamae5.svg" class="image"><img alt="" style="width:32px;max-width:32px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Busyu-kamae6.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Busyu-kamae6.svg/30px-Busyu-kamae6.svg.png" decoding="async" width="30" height="30" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Busyu-kamae6.svg/45px-Busyu-kamae6.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Busyu-kamae6.svg/60px-Busyu-kamae6.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="8" data-file-height="8" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="background-color:transparent">Structures of compounds, with red marked positions of <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_characters)" title="Radical (Chinese characters)">radicals</a></div></div></div></div>
<p><i>Semantic-phonetic compounds</i> or <i>pictophonetic compounds</i> are by far the most numerous characters. These characters are composed of at least two parts. A semantic component, often a character key as a clue to the topic to which the character refers, often in an abbreviated form, The <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantic</a> component suggests the general meaning of the compound character. The  <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetic</a> component suggests the pronunciation of the compound character. In most cases the semantic indicator is also the 部首 <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_characters)" title="Radical (Chinese characters)">radical</a> under which the character is listed in dictionaries. Because Chinese is replete in homophones phonetic elements may also carry semantic content. In some rare examples phono-semantic characters may also convey pictorial content. Each Chinese character is an attempt to combine sound, image, and idea in a mutually reinforcing fashion.
</p><p>Examples of phono-semantic characters include <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">河</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">hé</i></i> "river", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">湖</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">hú</i></i> "lake", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">流</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">liú</i></i> "stream", <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">沖</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">chōng</i></i> "surge", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">滑</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">huá</i></i> "slippery". All these characters have on the left a radical of three short strokes (氵), which is a reduced form of the character 水 <i>shuǐ</i> meaning "water", indicating that the character has a semantic connection with water. The right-hand side in each case is a phonetic indicator. For example, in the case of <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">沖</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">chōng</i></i> (Old Chinese <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">*ɡ-ljuŋ</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992750_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992750-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>) "surge", the phonetic indicator is <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">中</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">zhōng</i></i> (Old Chinese <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">*k-ljuŋ</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992810_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992810-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>), which by itself means "middle". In this case it can be seen that the pronunciation of the character is slightly different from that of its phonetic indicator; the effect of historical sound change means that the composition of such characters can sometimes seem arbitrary today.
</p><p>In general, phonetic components do not determine the exact pronunciation of a character, but only give a clue to a its pronunciation. While some characters take the exact pronunciation of their phonetic component, others take only the initial or final sounds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In fact, some characters' pronunciations may not correspond to the pronunciations of their phonetic parts at all, which is sometimes the case with characters after having undergone simplification. The 8 characters in the following table all take 也 for their phonetic part, however, as it is readily apparent, none of them take the pronunciation of 也, which is yě (Old Chinese *lajʔ).  As the table below shows, the sound changes that have taken place since the Shang/Zhou period when most of these characters were created can be dramatic, to the point of not providing any useful hint of the modern pronunciation.
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable">

<p>Xu Shen (c. 100&#160;AD) placed approximately 82% of characters into this category, while in the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Dictionary" title="Kangxi Dictionary">Kangxi Dictionary</a> (1716&#160;AD) the number is closer to 90%, due to the extremely productive use of this technique to extend the Chinese vocabulary.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chu_Nom" class="mw-redirect" title="Chu Nom">Chu Nom</a> characters of Vietnam were created using this principle.
</p><p>This method is used to form new characters, for example <span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">钚</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">鈈</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">bù</i></i> ("<a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>") is the metal radical <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">金</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">jīn</i></i> plus the phonetic component <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">不</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">bù</i></i>, described in Chinese as "<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">不</span> gives sound, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">金</span> gives meaning". Many <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table_in_East_Asian_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Periodic table in East Asian languages">Chinese names of elements in the periodic table</a> and many other <a href="/wiki/Organic_nomenclature_in_Chinese" title="Organic nomenclature in Chinese">chemistry-related characters</a> were formed this way. In fact, it is possible to tell from a Chinese periodic table at a glance which elements are metal (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">金</span>), solid nonmetal (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">石</span>, "stone"), liquid (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">氵</span>), or gas (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">气</span>).
</p><p>Occasionally a bisyllabic word is written with two characters that contain the same radical, as in <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">蝴蝶</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">húdié</i></i> "butterfly", where both characters have the insect radical <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">虫</span>. A notable example is <a href="/wiki/Pipa" title="Pipa">pipa</a> (a Chinese lute, also a fruit, the <a href="/wiki/Loquat" title="Loquat">loquat</a>, of similar shape) – originally written as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">批把</span> with the hand radical (扌), referring to the down and up strokes when playing this instrument, which was then changed to <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">枇杷</span> (tree radical <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">木</span>), which is still used for the fruit, while the character was changed to <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">琵琶</span> when referring to the instrument (radical <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">玨</span>) .<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In other cases a compound word may coincidentally share a radical without this being meaningful.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transformed_cognates">Transformed cognates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Transformed cognates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">转注字</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">轉注字</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">zhuǎnzhùzì</i></i></li></ul>
<p>The smallest category of characters is also the least understood.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198869_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198869-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In the postface to the <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">Shuowen Jiezi</i></i>, Xu Shen gave as an example the characters <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">考</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">kǎo</i></i> "to verify" and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">老</span> <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">lǎo</i></i> "old", which had similar Old Chinese pronunciations (*khuʔ and *C-ruʔ respectively<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992771,_772_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992771,_772-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>) and may once have been the same word, meaning "elderly person", but became <a href="/wiki/Lexicalization" title="Lexicalization">lexicalized</a> into two separate words. The term does not appear in the body of the dictionary, and is often omitted from modern systems.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013260–261_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013260–261-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform,_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg/220px-Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="341" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg/330px-Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg/440px-Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg 2x" data-file-width="875" data-file-height="1355" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform,_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparative evolution from pictograms to abstract shapes, in cuneiform, Egyptian and Chinese characters</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legendary_origins">Legendary origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Legendary origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to legend, Chinese characters were invented by <a href="/wiki/Cangjie" title="Cangjie">Cangjie</a>, a bureaucrat under the legendary <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Yellow Emperor</a>. Inspired by his study of the animals of the world, the landscape of the earth and the stars in the sky, Cangjie is said to have invented symbols called <i><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" title="Chinese language text">zì</i></i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">字</span>) – the first Chinese characters. The legend relates that on the day the characters were created, grain rained down from the sky and that night the people heard ghosts wailing and demons crying because the human beings could no longer be cheated.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_sign_use">Early sign use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Early sign use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_signs_in_China" title="Neolithic signs in China">Neolithic signs in China</a></div>
<p>In recent decades, a series of inscribed graphs and pictures have been found at <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> sites in China, including <a href="/wiki/Jiahu" title="Jiahu">Jiahu</a> (c. 6500&#160;BC), <a href="/wiki/Dadiwan_culture" title="Dadiwan culture">Dadiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damaidi" title="Damaidi">Damaidi</a> from the 6th millennium&#160;BC, and <a href="/wiki/Banpo" title="Banpo">Banpo</a> (5th millennium&#160;BC).  Often these finds are accompanied by media reports that push back the purported beginnings of Chinese writing by thousands of years.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rincon_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincon-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> However, because these marks occur singly, without any implied context, and are made crudely and simply, <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Xigui" title="Qiu Xigui">Qiu Xigui</a> concluded that "we do not have any basis for stating that these constituted writing nor is there reason to conclude that they were ancestral to <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> Chinese characters."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu200031_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu200031-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> They do however demonstrate a history of sign use in the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> valley during the Neolithic through to the Shang period.<sup id="cite_ref-rincon_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rincon-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oracle_bone_script">Oracle bone script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Oracle bone script">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">Oracle bone script</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg/170px-Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg/255px-Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg/340px-Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1733" data-file-height="2669" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ox scapula with oracle bone inscription</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest confirmed evidence of the Chinese script yet discovered is the body of inscriptions carved on  bronze vessels and <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone" title="Oracle bone">oracle bones</a> from the late <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> (c. 1250–1050&#160;BC).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20101_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20101-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeightley1978xvi_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeightley1978xvi-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The earliest of these is dated to around 1200&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Bagley_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bagley-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boltz_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boltz-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In 1899, pieces of these bones were being sold as "dragon bones" for medicinal purposes, when scholars identified the symbols on them as Chinese writing. By 1928, the source of the bones had been traced to a village near <a href="/wiki/Anyang" title="Anyang">Anyang</a> in <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan Province</a>, which was excavated by the <a href="/wiki/Academia_Sinica" title="Academia Sinica">Academia Sinica</a> between 1928 and 1937. Over 150,000 fragments have been found.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20101_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20101-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oracle bone inscriptions are records of divinations performed in communication with royal ancestral spirits.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20101_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20101-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The shortest are only a few characters long, while the longest are thirty to forty characters in length. The Shang king would communicate with his ancestors on topics relating to the royal family, military success, weather forecasting, ritual sacrifices, and related topics by means of <a href="/wiki/Scapulimancy" title="Scapulimancy">scapulimancy</a>, and the answers would be recorded on the divination material itself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20101_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20101-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Oracle-bone_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Oracle-bone script">oracle-bone script</a> is a well-developed writing system,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoltz1986424_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoltz1986424-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeightley1996_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeightley1996-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> suggesting that the Chinese script's origins may lie earlier than the late second millennium&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20102_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20102-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>  Although these divinatory inscriptions are the earliest surviving evidence of ancient Chinese writing, it is widely believed that writing was used for many other non-official purposes, but that the materials upon which non-divinatory writing was done – likely wood and bamboo – were less durable than bone and shell and have since decayed away.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20102_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20102-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age:_parallel_script_forms_and_gradual_evolution">Bronze Age: parallel script forms and gradual evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bronze Age: parallel script forms and gradual evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">Chinese bronze inscriptions</a></div><p>
The traditional picture of an orderly series of scripts, each one invented suddenly and then completely displacing the previous one, has been conclusively demonstrated to be fiction by the archaeological finds and scholarly research of the later 20th and early 21st centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu200063–64,_66,_86,_88–89,_104–107,_124_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu200063–64,_66,_86,_88–89,_104–107,_124-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Gradual evolution and the coexistence of two or more scripts was more often the case. As early as the Shang dynasty, oracle-bone script coexisted as a simplified form alongside the normal script of <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> books (preserved in typical <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">bronze inscriptions</a>), as well as the extra-elaborate pictorial forms (often clan emblems) found on many bronzes.</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_Zhou_Ritual_Containers3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Western_Zhou_Ritual_Containers3.jpg/220px-Western_Zhou_Ritual_Containers3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Western_Zhou_Ritual_Containers3.jpg/330px-Western_Zhou_Ritual_Containers3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Western_Zhou_Ritual_Containers3.jpg/440px-Western_Zhou_Ritual_Containers3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1965" data-file-height="1260" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_Zhou_Ritual_Containers3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Left: Bronze fāngzūn (<span lang="zh"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%96%B9" class="extiw" title="wikt:方">方</a><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%A8%BD" class="extiw" title="wikt:樽">樽</a></span>) ritual wine container dated about 1000&#160;BC. The inscription cast in bronze on the vessel commemorates a <a href="/wiki/Shell_money" title="Shell money">gift of cowrie shells</a> (then used as currency in China) from someone of presumably elite status in <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> society. Right: Bronze fāngyí (<span lang="zh"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%96%B9" class="extiw" title="wikt:方">方</a><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%BD%9D" class="extiw" title="wikt:彝">彝</a></span>) ritual container dated about 1000&#160;BC. An inscription of some 180 Chinese characters appears twice on the vessel. The inscription comments on state rituals that accompanied court ceremony, recorded by an official scribe.</div></div></div><p> Based on studies of these bronze inscriptions, it is clear that, from the Shang dynasty writing to that of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Zhou" title="Eastern Zhou">Eastern Zhou</a>, the mainstream script evolved in a slow, unbroken fashion, until assuming the form that is now known as <a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">seal script</a> in the late Eastern Zhou in the state of <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Qin</a>, without any clear line of division.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu200059–150_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu200059–150-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, other scripts had evolved, especially in the eastern and southern areas during the late <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a>, including regional forms, such as the <i>gǔwén</i> ("ancient forms") of the eastern <a href="/wiki/Warring_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Warring States">Warring States</a> preserved as variant forms in the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> character dictionary <i><a href="/wiki/Shuowen_Jiezi" title="Shuowen Jiezi">Shuowen Jiezi</a></i>, as well as decorative forms such as <a href="/wiki/Bird-worm_seal_script" title="Bird-worm seal script">bird and insect scripts</a>.
</p><h3><span id="Unification:_seal_script.2C_vulgar_writing_and_proto-clerical"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Unification:_seal_script,_vulgar_writing_and_proto-clerical">Unification: seal script, vulgar writing and proto-clerical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Unification: seal script, vulgar writing and proto-clerical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em decoding="async" width="120" height="122" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/1798_%E1%83%AC._%E1%83%92%E1%83%98%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A0%E1%83%92%E1%83%98_XII-%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%90%E1%83%96%E1%83%9C%E1%83%90%E1%83%A3%E1%83%A0%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A8%E1%83%95%E1%83%98%E1%83%9A%E1%83%9D%E1%83%91%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%AC%E1%83%A7%E1%83%90%E1%83%9A%E1%83%9D%E1%83%91%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%A1%E1%83%98%E1%83%92%E1%83%94%E1%83%9A%E1%83%98.jpg/180px-1798_%E1%83%AC._%E1%83%92%E1%83%98%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A0%E1%83%92%E1%83%98_XII-%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%90%E1%83%96%E1%83%9C%E1%83%90%E1%83%A3%E1%83%A0%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A8%E1%83%95%E1%83%98%E1%83%9A%E1%83%9D%E1%83%91%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%AC%E1%83%A7%E1%83%90%E1%83%9A%E1%83%9D%E1%83%91%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%A1%E1%83%98%E1%83%92%E1%83%94%E1%83%9A%E1%83%98.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/1798_%E1%83%AC._%E1%83%92%E1%83%98%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A0%E1%83%92%E1%83%98_XII-%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%90%E1%83%96%E1%83%9C%E1%83%90%E1%83%A3%E1%83%A0%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A8%E1%83%95%E1%83%98%E1%83%9A%E1%83%9D%E1%83%91%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%AC%E1%83%A7%E1%83%90%E1%83%9A%E1%83%9D%E1%83%91%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%A1%E1%83%98%E1%83%92%E1%83%94%E1%83%9A%E1%83%98.jpg/240px-1798_%E1%83%AC._%E1%83%92%E1%83%98%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A0%E1%83%92%E1%83%98_XII-%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%90%E1%83%96%E1%83%9C%E1%83%90%E1%83%A3%E1%83%A0%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A8%E1%83%95%E1%83%98%E1%83%9A%E1%83%9D%E1%83%91%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%AC%E1%83%A7%E1%83%90%E1%83%9A%E1%83%9D%E1%83%91%E1%83%98%E1%83%A1_%E1%83%A1%E1%83%98%E1%83%92%E1%83%94%E1%83%9A%E1%83%98.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="406" /></a></td></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">Calligraphy</a></th></tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-left:1.5em;padding-right:1.5em;">

<li><a href="/wiki/Georgian_calligraphy" title="Georgian calligraphy">Georgian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Indian_calligraphy" title="Indian calligraphy">Indian</a></li>
