<li><a href="/wiki/Japanese_calligraphy" title="Japanese calligraphy">Japanese</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Korean_calligraphy" title="Korean calligraphy">Korean</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mongolian_calligraphy" title="Mongolian calligraphy">Mongolian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Persian_calligraphy" title="Persian calligraphy">Persian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tibetan_calligraphy" title="Tibetan calligraphy">Tibetan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Western_calligraphy" title="Western calligraphy">Western</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Calligraphy" title="Template:Calligraphy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Calligraphy" title="Template talk:Calligraphy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Calligraphy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">Seal script</a>, which had evolved slowly in the state of Qin during the Eastern <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a>, became standardized and adopted as the formal script for all of China in the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> (leading to a popular misconception that it was invented at that time), and was still widely used for decorative engraving and <a href="/wiki/Seal_(device)" class="mw-redirect" title="Seal (device)">seals</a> (name chops, or signets) in the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> period. However, despite the Qin script standardization, more than one script remained in use at the time. For example, a little-known, rectilinear and roughly executed kind of common (vulgar) writing had for centuries coexisted with the more formal seal script in the <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Qin state</a>, and the popularity of this vulgar writing grew as the use of writing itself became more widespread.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000104_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000104-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> By the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>, an immature form of <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a> called "early clerical" or "proto-clerical" had already developed in the state of Qin<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu200059,_104–107_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu200059,_104–107-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> based upon this vulgar writing, and with influence from seal script as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000119_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000119-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The coexistence of the three scripts – small seal, vulgar and proto-clerical, with the latter evolving gradually in the Qin to early Han dynasties into <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a> – runs counter to the traditional belief that the Qin dynasty had one script only, and that clerical script was suddenly invented in the early Han dynasty from the <a href="/wiki/Small_seal_script" title="Small seal script">small seal script</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Han_dynasty">Han dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Han dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proto-clerical_evolving_to_clerical">Proto-clerical evolving to clerical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Proto-clerical evolving to clerical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Proto-clerical script, which had emerged by the time of the Warring States period from vulgar Qin writing, matured gradually, and by the early Western Han period, it was little different from that of the Qin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000123_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000123-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Recently discovered bamboo slips show the script becoming mature clerical script by the middle-to-late reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu of the Western Han</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000119,_123–124_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000119,_123–124-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> who ruled from 141 to 87&#160;BC.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Clerical_and_clerical_cursive">Clerical and clerical cursive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Clerical and clerical cursive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Contrary to the popular belief of there being only one script per period, there were in fact multiple scripts in use during the Han period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000130_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000130-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Although mature <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a>, also called <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">八分</span> (bāfēn)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000121_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000121-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> script, was dominant at that time, an early type of <a href="/wiki/Cursive_script_(East_Asia)" title="Cursive script (East Asia)">cursive script</a> was also in use by the Han by at least as early as 24&#160;BC (during the very late Western Han period),<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> incorporating cursive forms popular at the time, well as many elements from the vulgar writing of the <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Warring State of Qin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000131,_133_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000131,_133-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> By around the time of the Eastern <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jin dynasty (265–420)">Jin dynasty</a>, this Han cursive became known as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">章草</span> <i>zhāngcǎo</i> (also known as <span lang="zh-Hans">隶草</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant">隸草</span> <i>lìcǎo</i> today), or in English sometimes clerical cursive, ancient cursive, or draft cursive. Some believe that the name, based on <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">章</span> <i>zhāng</i> meaning "orderly", arose because the script was a more orderly form<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> of cursive than <a href="/wiki/Cursive_script_(East_Asia)" title="Cursive script (East Asia)">the modern form</a>, which emerged during the Eastern <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jin dynasty (265–420)">Jin dynasty</a> and is still in use today, called <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">今草</span> <i>jīncǎo</i> or "modern cursive".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000131_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000131-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-clerical">Neo-clerical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Neo-clerical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Around the mid-<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a> period,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> a simplified and easier-to-write form of clerical script appeared, which Qiu terms "neo-clerical" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">新隶体</span> / <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">新隸體</span>, <i>xīnlìtǐ</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000113,_139_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000113,_139-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> By the late Eastern Han, this had become the dominant daily script,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> although the formal, mature <i>bāfēn</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">八分</span>) clerical script remained in use for formal works such as engraved <a href="/wiki/Stelae" class="mw-redirect" title="Stelae">stelae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Qiu describes this neo-clerical script as a transition between clerical and regular script,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138_65-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and it remained in use through the <a href="/wiki/Cao_Wei" title="Cao Wei">Cao Wei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jin dynasty (265–420)">Jin dynasties</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000139_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000139-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Semi-cursive">Semi-cursive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Semi-cursive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By the late Eastern Han period, an early form of <a href="/wiki/Semi-cursive_script" title="Semi-cursive script">semi-cursive script</a> appeared,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000113,_139_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000113,_139-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> developing out of a cursively written form of neo-clerical script<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> and simple cursive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000142_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000142-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> This semi-cursive script was traditionally attributed to Liu Desheng c. 147–188&#160;AD,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000139_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000139-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> although such attributions refer to early masters of a script rather than to their actual inventors, since the scripts generally evolved into being over time. Qiu gives examples of early semi-cursive script, showing that it had popular origins rather than being purely Liu's invention.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000140_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000140-72">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wei_to_Jin_period">Wei to Jin period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Wei to Jin period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regular_script">Regular script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Regular script">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">Regular script</a> has been attributed to <a href="/wiki/Zhong_Yao" title="Zhong Yao">Zhong Yao</a> (c. 151–230&#160;AD), during the period at the <a href="/wiki/End_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="End of the Han dynasty">end of the Han dynasty</a> in the state of <a href="/wiki/Cao_Wei" title="Cao Wei">Cao Wei</a>. Zhong Yao has been called the "father of regular script". However, some scholars<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> postulate that one person alone could not have developed a new script which was universally adopted, but could only have been a contributor to its gradual formation. The earliest surviving pieces written in regular script are copies of Zhong Yao's works, including at least one copied by <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a>. This new script, which is the dominant modern Chinese script, developed out of a neatly written form of early semi-cursive, with addition of the pause (<span lang="zh-Hans">顿</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">頓</span> <i>dùn</i>) technique to end horizontal strokes, plus heavy tails on strokes which are written to the downward-right diagonal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000143_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000143-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Thus, early regular script emerged from a neat, formal form of semi-cursive,  which had itself emerged from neo-clerical (a simplified, convenient form of clerical script). It then matured further in the Eastern <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jin dynasty (265–420)">Jin dynasty</a> in the hands of the "Sage of Calligraphy", <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a>, and his son <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xianzhi_(calligrapher)" title="Wang Xianzhi (calligrapher)">Wang Xianzhi</a>. It was not, however, in widespread use at that time, and most writers continued using neo-clerical, or a somewhat semi-cursive form of it, for daily writing,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000143_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000143-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> while the conservative bafen clerical script remained in use on some stelae, alongside some semi-cursive, but primarily neo-clerical.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000144_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000144-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_cursive">Modern cursive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Modern cursive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Meanwhile, modern cursive script slowly emerged from the clerical cursive (<i>zhāngcǎo</i>) script during the Cao Wei to Jin period, under the influence of both semi-cursive and the newly emerged regular script.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000148_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000148-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Cursive was formalized in the hands of a few master calligraphers, the most famous and influential of whom was <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dominance_and_maturation_of_regular_script">Dominance and maturation of regular script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Dominance and maturation of regular script">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It was not until the <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Northern and Southern dynasties</a> that regular script rose to dominant status.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000145_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000145-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> During that period, regular script continued evolving stylistically, reaching full maturity in the early <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>. Some call the writing of the early Tang calligrapher <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xun" title="Ouyang Xun">Ouyang Xun</a> (557–641) the first mature regular script. After this point, although developments in the art of calligraphy and in character simplification still lay ahead, there were no more major stages of evolution for the mainstream script.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although most of the simplified Chinese characters in use today are the result of the works moderated by the government of the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> in the 1950s and 60s, character simplification predates the republic's formation in 1949.
One of the earliest proponents of character simplification was <a href="/wiki/Lufei_Kui" title="Lufei Kui">Lufei Kui</a>, who proposed in 1909 that simplified characters should be used in education. In the years following the <a href="/wiki/May_Fourth_Movement" title="May Fourth Movement">May Fourth Movement</a> in 1919, many anti-imperialist Chinese intellectuals sought ways to modernise China. In the 1930s and 1940s, discussions on character simplification took place within the <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a> government, and many Chinese intellectuals and writers have long maintained that character simplification would help boost literacy in China. In many world languages, literacy has been promoted as a justification for <a href="/wiki/Spelling_reform" title="Spelling reform">spelling reforms</a>. The People's Republic of China issued its first round of official character simplifications in two documents, the first in 1956 and the second in 1964. In the 1950s and 1960s, while confusion about simplified characters was still rampant, transitional characters that mixed simplified parts with yet-to-be simplified parts of characters together appeared briefly, then disappeared.
</p><p>"<a href="/wiki/Han_unification" title="Han unification">Han unification</a>" was an effort by the authors of Unicode and the Universal Character Set to map multiple character sets of the so-called CJK languages (Chinese/Japanese/Korean) into a single set of unified characters and was completed for the purposes of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> in 1991 (Unicode&#160;1.0).
</p><p>Apart from Chinese ones, Korean, Japanese and Vietnamese normative medium of record-keeping, written historical narratives and official communication are in adaptations and variations of Chinese script.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptation_to_other_languages">Adaptation to other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Adaptation to other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png/220px-Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png/330px-Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png/440px-Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png 2x" data-file-width="1971" data-file-height="1269" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Current and former extension of the use of Chinese characters</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_family_of_scripts" title="Chinese family of scripts">Chinese family of scripts</a></div>
<p>The Chinese script spread to <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> together with <a href="/wiki/Korean_Buddhism" title="Korean Buddhism">Buddhism</a> from the 2nd century&#160;BC to 5th century&#160;AD (<a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> This was adopted for recording the Japanese language from the 5th century&#160;AD.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chinese characters were first used in Vietnam during the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_domination_of_Vietnam" title="Chinese domination of Vietnam">millennium of Chinese rule</a> starting in 111&#160;BC. They were used to write Classical Chinese and adapted around the 13th century to create the <a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_N%C3%B4m" title="Chữ Nôm">Nôm</a> script to write Vietnamese.
</p><p>Currently, the only non-Chinese language outside of China that regularly uses the Chinese script is Japanese. Vietnam abandoned their use in the early 20th&#160;century in favour of a Latin-based script, and Korea in the late 20th century in favour of its homegrown <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">hangul</a> script, although as Korea switched much more recently, many Koreans still learn them to read texts written before then, or in some cases to disambiguate homophones.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese">Japanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Japanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">Kanji</a></div>
<p>Chinese characters adapted to write <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> words are known as <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a>. Chinese words borrowed into Japanese could be written with Chinese characters, while native Japanese words could also be written using the character(s) for a Chinese word of similar meaning. Most kanji have both the native (and often multi-syllabic) Japanese pronunciation, known as kun'yomi, and the (mono-syllabic) Chinese-based pronunciation, known as on'yomi. For example, the native Japanese word <i>katana</i> is written as <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">刀</span></span> in kanji, which uses the native pronunciation since the word is native to Japanese, while the Chinese loanword <i>nihontō</i> (meaning "Japanese sword") is written as <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">日本刀</span></span>, which uses the Chinese-based pronunciation. While nowadays loanwords from non-Sinosphere languages are usually just written in <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a>, one of the two syllabary systems of Japanese, loanwords that were borrowed into Japanese before the Meiji Period were typically written with Chinese characters whose on'yomi had the same pronunciation as the loanword itself, words like <i>Amerika</i> (kanji: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">亜米利加</span></span>, katakana: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">アメリカ</span></span>, meaning: America), <i>karuta</i> (kanji: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">歌留多</span></span>, <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">加留多</span></span>, katakana: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">カルタ</span></span>, meaning: card, letter), and <i>tempura</i> (kanji: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">天婦羅</span></span>, <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">天麩羅</span></span>, katakana: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">テンプラ</span></span>, meaning: tempura), although the meanings of the characters used often had no relation to the words themselves. Kanji that are used to only represent the sounds of a word are called <a href="/wiki/Ateji" title="Ateji">ateji</a>. While foreign loanwords in Japanese words are usually written only in kana, there are some words that normally use ateji to this day, like <i>kurabu</i> (ateji: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">俱楽部</span></span>, katakana: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">クラブ</span></span>, meaning: club) and <i>sushi</i> (ateji: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">寿司</span></span>, katakana: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">スシ</span></span>).
Because there have been multiple layers of borrowing into Japanese, a single character may have several readings in Japanese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991122–129_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991122–129-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Written Japanese also includes a pair of <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabaries</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Kana" title="Kana">kana</a>, derived by simplifying Chinese characters selected to represent syllables of Japanese.
The syllabaries differ because they sometimes selected different characters for a syllable, and because they used different strategies to reduce these characters for easy writing: the angular <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> were obtained by selecting a part of each character, while <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a> were derived from the cursive forms of whole characters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991129–132_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991129–132-83">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
Modern Japanese writing uses a composite system, using kanji for <a href="/wiki/Word_stem" title="Word stem">word stems</a>, hiragana for inflexional endings and grammatical words, and katakana to transcribe non-Chinese loanwords as well as serve as a method to emphasize native words (similar to how italics are used in Romance languages).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991132–133_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991132–133-84">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean">Korean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Korean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a></div>
<p>In times past, until the 15th century, in Korea, Literary Chinese was the dominant form of written communication prior to the creation of <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">hangul</a>, the Korean alphabet. Much of the vocabulary, especially in the realms of science and sociology, comes directly from Chinese, comparable to Latin or Greek root words in European languages. However, due to the lack of tones in Modern Standard Korean,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIto_&amp;_Kenstowicz2017_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIto_&amp;_Kenstowicz2017-85">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>  as the words were imported from Chinese, many dissimilar characters and syllables took on identical pronunciations, and subsequently identical spelling in hangul.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  Chinese characters are sometimes used to this day for either clarification in a practical manner, or to give a distinguished appearance, as knowledge of Chinese characters is considered by many Koreans a high class attribute and an indispensable part of a classical education.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It is also observed that the preference for Chinese characters is treated as being conservative and Confucian.
</p><p>In Korea, <i><a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a></i> have become a politically contentious issue, with some Koreans urging a "purification" of the national language and culture by totally abandoning their use. These individuals encourage the exclusive use of the native hangul alphabet throughout Korean society and the end to character education in public schools.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Other Koreans support the revival of Hanja in everyday usage, like in the 1970s and 80s.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In South Korea, educational policy on characters has swung back and forth, often swayed by education ministers' personal opinions. At present, middle and high school students (grades 7 to 12) are taught 1,800 characters,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHannas199768–72_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHannas199768–72-86">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> albeit with the principal focus on recognition, with the aim of achieving newspaper literacy.
</p><p>There is a clear trend toward the exclusive use of hangul in day-to-day South Korean society. Hanja are still used to some extent, particularly in newspapers, weddings, place names and <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a> (although it is nowhere near the extent of kanji use in day-to-day Japanese society). Hanja is also extensively used in situations where ambiguity must be avoided,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> such as academic papers, high-level corporate reports, government documents, and newspapers; this is due to the large number of <a href="/wiki/Homonyms" class="mw-redirect" title="Homonyms">homonyms</a> that have resulted from <a href="/wiki/Sino-Korean_vocabulary" title="Sino-Korean vocabulary">extensive borrowing</a> of Chinese words.
</p><p>The issue of ambiguity is the main hurdle in any effort to "cleanse" the Korean language of Chinese characters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Characters convey meaning visually, while alphabets convey guidance to pronunciation, which in turn hints at meaning. As an example, in Korean dictionaries, the phonetic entry for <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">기사</span> <i>gisa</i> yields more than 30 different entries. In the past, this ambiguity had been efficiently resolved by parenthetically displaying the associated hanja. While hanja is sometimes used for Sino-Korean vocabulary, native Korean words are rarely, if ever, written in hanja.
</p><p>When learning how to write hanja, students are taught to memorize the native Korean pronunciation for the hanja's meaning and the Sino-Korean pronunciations (the pronunciation based on the Chinese pronunciation of the characters) for each hanja respectively so that students know what the syllable and meaning is for a particular hanja. For example, the name for the hanja <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">水</span> is <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">물</span> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">수</span> (mul-su) in which <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">물</span> (mul) is the native Korean pronunciation for "water", while <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">수</span> (su) is the Sino-Korean pronunciation of the character. The naming of hanja is similar to if "water" were named "water-aqua", "horse-equus", or "gold-aurum" based on a hybridization of both the English and the Latin names. Other examples include <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">사람</span> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">인</span> (saram-in) for <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">人</span> "person/people", <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">큰</span> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">대</span> (keun-dae) for <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">大</span> "big/large//great", <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">작을</span> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">소</span> (jakeul-so) for <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">小</span> "small/little", <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">아래</span> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">하</span> (arae-ha) for <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">下</span> "underneath/below/low", <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">아비</span> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">부</span> (abi-bu) for <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">父</span> "father", and <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">나라이름</span> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">한</span> (naraireum-han) for <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">韓</span> "Han/Korea".<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In North Korea, the <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a> system was once completely banned since June 1949 due to fears of collapsed containment of the country; during the 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Kim_Il_Sung" class="mw-redirect" title="Kim Il Sung">Kim Il Sung</a> had condemned all sorts of foreign languages (even the newly proposed <a href="/wiki/New_Korean_Orthography" title="New Korean Orthography">New Korean Orthography</a>). The ban continued into the 21st century. However, a textbook for university history departments containing 3,323 distinct characters was published in 1971.  In the 1990s, school children were still expected to learn 2,000 characters (more than in South Korea or Japan).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHannas199768_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHannas199768-88">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/Kim_Jong_Il" class="mw-redirect" title="Kim Jong Il">Kim Jong Il</a>, the second ruler of North Korea, died in December 2011, <a href="/wiki/Kim_Jong_Un" class="mw-redirect" title="Kim Jong Un">Kim Jong Un</a> stepped up and began mandating the use of Hanja as a source of definition for the Korean language. Currently, it is said that North Korea teaches around 3,000 Hanja characters to North Korean students, and in some cases, the characters appear within advertisements and newspapers. However, it is also said that the authorities implore students not to use the characters in public.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Due to North Korea's strict isolationism, accurate reports about hanja use in North Korea are hard to obtain.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Okinawan">Okinawan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Okinawan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Okinawan_language" title="Okinawan language">Okinawan language</a></div>
<p>Chinese characters are thought to have been first introduced to the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> in 1265 by a Japanese Buddhist monk.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> After the Okinawan kingdoms became tributaries of <a href="/wiki/Ming_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Ming China">Ming China</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Kingdom" title="Ryukyu Kingdom">Ryukyu Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical Chinese language">Classical Chinese</a> was used in court documents, but <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a> was mostly used for popular writing and poetry. After Ryukyu became a vassal of Japan's <a href="/wiki/Satsuma_Domain" title="Satsuma Domain">Satsuma Domain</a>, Chinese characters became more popular, as well as the use of <a href="/wiki/Kanbun" title="Kanbun">Kanbun</a>. In modern Okinawan, which is labeled as a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese dialect">Japanese dialect</a> by the Japanese government, <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> and hiragana are mostly used to write Okinawan, but Chinese characters are still used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnamese">Vietnamese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Vietnamese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_N%C3%B4m" title="Chữ Nôm">Chữ Nôm</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing_in_Vietnam" title="History of writing in Vietnam">History of writing in Vietnam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Han-nom_parallel-2.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Han-nom_parallel-2.svg/330px-Han-nom_parallel-2.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="56" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Han-nom_parallel-2.svg/495px-Han-nom_parallel-2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Han-nom_parallel-2.svg/660px-Han-nom_parallel-2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1300" data-file-height="220" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Han-nom_parallel-2.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"My mother eats vegetarian food at the pagoda every Sunday", written in the modern Vietnamese alphabet (blue) and Nom. Characters borrowed unchanged from Chinese are shown in green, while invented characters are brown.</div></div></div>
<p>Although Chinese characters in Vietnam are now limited to ceremonial uses, they were once in widespread use. Until the early 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Literary_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Literary Chinese">Literary Chinese</a> was used in Vietnam for all official and scholarly writing. Around the 13th century the <i>Nôm</i> script was developed to record folk literature in the Vietnamese language. The script used Chinese characters to represent both borrowed <a href="/wiki/Sino-Vietnamese_vocabulary" title="Sino-Vietnamese vocabulary">Sino-Vietnamese vocabulary</a> and native words with similar pronunciation or meaning. In addition thousands of new compound characters were created to write Vietnamese words. This process resulted in a highly complex system that was never mastered by more than 5% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Both Literary Chinese and <i>Nôm</i> were replaced in the early 20th century by Vietnamese written with the Latin-based <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_alphabet" title="Vietnamese alphabet">Vietnamese alphabet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991113–115_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991113–115-92">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis1997_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis1997-93">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_languages">Other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several minority languages of south and southwest China were formerly written with scripts based on Hanzi but also including many locally created characters.
The most extensive is the <i><a href="/wiki/Sawndip" title="Sawndip">sawndip</a></i> script for the <a href="/wiki/Zhuang_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhuang language">Zhuang language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a> which is still used to this day.
Other languages written with such scripts include <a href="/wiki/Hmongic_languages" title="Hmongic languages">Miao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mienic_languages" title="Mienic languages">Yao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bouyei_language#Ancient_Bouyei_script" title="Bouyei language">Bouyei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mulam_language" title="Mulam language">Mulam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kam_language" title="Kam language">Kam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bai_language" title="Bai language">Bai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hani_language" title="Hani language">Hani</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zhou_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhou-94">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> All these languages are now officially written using Latin-based scripts, while Chinese characters are still used for the Mulam language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Zhou Youguang article does not mention Mulam (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Even today for Zhuang, according to survey, the traditional sawndip script has twice as many users as the official Latin script.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The foreign dynasties that ruled northern China between the 10th and 13th centuries developed scripts that were inspired by Hanzi but did not use them directly: the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_large_script" title="Khitan large script">Khitan large script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khitan_small_script" title="Khitan small script">Khitan small script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tangut_script" title="Tangut script">Tangut script</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_script" title="Jurchen script">Jurchen script</a>.
Other scripts in China that borrowed or adapted a few Chinese characters but are otherwise distinct include <a href="/wiki/Geba_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Geba script">Geba script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sui_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Sui script">Sui script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yi_script" title="Yi script">Yi script</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lisu_language#Lisu_syllabary" title="Lisu language">Lisu syllabary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zhou_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhou-94">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transcription_of_foreign_languages">Transcription of foreign languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Transcription of foreign languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transcription_into_Chinese_characters" title="Transcription into Chinese characters">Transcription into Chinese characters</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Secret_history.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Secret_history.jpg/220px-Secret_history.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="326" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Secret_history.jpg/330px-Secret_history.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Secret_history.jpg/440px-Secret_history.jpg 2x" data-file-width="607" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Secret_history.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mongolian text from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a></i> in Chinese transcription, with a glossary on the right of each row</div></div></div>
<p>Along with <a href="/wiki/Persian_alphabet" title="Persian alphabet">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic</a>, Chinese characters were also used as a foreign script to write the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian language</a>, where characters were used to phonetically transcribe Mongolian sounds. Most notably, the only surviving copies of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a></i> were written in such a manner; the Chinese characters <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">忙豁侖紐察 脫[卜]察安</span> (pinyin: <i>mánghuōlúnniǔchá tuō[bo]chá'ān</i>) is the rendering of <i>Mongγol-un niγuca tobčiyan</i>, the title in Mongolian.
</p><p>Hanzi was also used to phonetically transcribe the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_language" title="Manchu language">Manchu language</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>.
</p><p>According to the Rev. John Gulick:  "The inhabitants of other Asiatic nations, who have had occasion to represent the words of their several languages by Chinese characters, have as a rule used unaspirated characters for the sounds, g, d, b. The Muslims from Arabia and Persia have followed this method … The Mongols, Manchu, and Japanese also constantly select unaspirated characters to represent the sounds g, d, b, and j of their languages. These surrounding Asiatic nations, in writing Chinese words in their own alphabets, have uniformly used g, d, b, etc., to represent the unaspirated sounds."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Simplification">Simplification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Simplification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified Chinese character">Simplified Chinese character</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_script_reform" title="Japanese script reform">Japanese script reform</a></div>
<p>Chinese character simplification is the overall reduction of the number of strokes in the <a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">regular script</a> of a set of Chinese characters.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Simplification_in_China">Simplification in China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Simplification in China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The use of traditional Chinese characters versus simplified Chinese characters varies greatly, and can depend on both the local customs and the medium. Before the official reform, character simplifications were not officially sanctioned and generally adopted vulgar variants and idiosyncratic substitutions. <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_variant" class="mw-redirect" title="Vulgar variant">Orthodox variants</a> were mandatory in printed works, while the (unofficial) simplified characters would be used in everyday writing or quick notes. Since the 1950s, and especially with the publication of the 1964 list, the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> has officially adopted <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese characters</a> for use in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, Macau, and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> (Taiwan) were not affected by the reform. There is no absolute rule for using either system, and often it is determined by what the target audience understands, as well as the upbringing of the writer.
</p><p>Although most often associated with the People's Republic of China, character simplification predates the 1949 communist victory. <a href="/wiki/Caoshu" class="mw-redirect" title="Caoshu">Caoshu</a>, cursive written text, almost always includes character simplification, and simplified forms have always existed in print, albeit not for the most formal works. In the 1930s and 1940s, discussions on character simplification took place within the <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a> government, and a large number of Chinese intellectuals and writers have long maintained that character simplification would help boost literacy in China. Indeed, this desire by the Kuomintang to simplify the Chinese writing system (inherited and implemented by the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party of China</a>) also nursed aspirations of some for the adoption of a phonetic script based on the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>, and spawned such inventions as the <a href="/wiki/Gwoyeu_Romatzyh" title="Gwoyeu Romatzyh">Gwoyeu Romatzyh</a>.
</p><p>The People's Republic of China issued its first round of official character simplifications in two documents, the first in 1956 and the second in 1964. A <a href="/wiki/Second-round_simplified_Chinese_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Second-round simplified Chinese character">second round of character simplifications</a> (known as <i>erjian</i>, or "second round simplified characters") was promulgated in 1977. It was poorly received, and in 1986 the authorities rescinded the second round completely, while making six revisions to the 1964 list, including the restoration of three traditional characters that had been simplified: <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">叠</span> <i>dié</i>, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">覆</span> <i>fù</i>, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">像</span> <i>xiàng</i>.
</p><p>The majority of simplified characters are drawn from conventional abbreviated forms, or ancient standard forms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey1987147_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey1987147-97">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> For example, the orthodox character <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">來</span> <i>lái</i> ("come") was written with the structure <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">来</span> in the <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">隶书</span> / <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">隸書</span>, <i>lìshū</i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>. This clerical form uses one fewer stroke, and was thus adopted as a simplified form. The character <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">雲</span> <i>yún</i> ("cloud") was written with the structure <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">云</span> in the <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">oracle bone script</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>, and had remained in use later as a phonetic loan in the meaning of "to say" while the <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">雨</span> <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_character)" class="mw-redirect" title="Radical (Chinese character)">radical</a> was added to differentiate meanings. The simplified form adopts the original structure.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Simplification_in_Japan">Simplification in Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Simplification in Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">Kanji</a>, <a href="/wiki/T%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Tōyō kanji">Tōyō kanji</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">Jōyō kanji</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shinjitai" title="Shinjitai">Shinjitai</a></div>
<p>In the years after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese government">Japanese government</a> also instituted a series of orthographic reforms. Some characters were given simplified forms called <i lang="ja-Latn" title="Hepburn transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Shinjitai" title="Shinjitai">shinjitai</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">新字体</span>, lit. "new character forms")</span>; the older forms were then labelled the <i lang="ja-Latn" title="Hepburn transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Ky%C5%ABjitai" title="Kyūjitai">kyūjitai</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">旧字体</span>, lit. "old character forms")</span>. The number of characters in common use was restricted, and formal lists of characters to be learned during each grade of school were established, first the 1850-character <i lang="ja-Latn" title="Hepburn transliteration"><a href="/wiki/T%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Tōyō kanji">tōyō kanji</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">当用漢字</span>)</span> list in 1945, the 1945-character <i lang="ja-Latn" title="Hepburn transliteration"><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">jōyō kanji</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">常用漢字</span>)</span> list in 1981, and a 2136-character reformed version of the <i>jōyō kanji</i> in 2010. Many variant forms of characters and obscure alternatives for common characters were officially discouraged. This was done with the goal of facilitating learning for children and simplifying kanji use in literature and periodicals. These are simply guidelines, hence many characters outside these standards are still widely known and commonly used, especially those used for personal and place names (for the latter, see <a href="/wiki/Jinmeiy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jinmeiyō kanji">jinmeiyō kanji</a>),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> as well as for some common words such as "dragon"<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">竜/龍</span>, <i lang="ja-Latn" title="Hepburn transliteration">tatsu</i>)</span> in which both old and new forms of the character are both acceptable and widely known amongst native Japanese speakers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asian_Chinese_communities">Southeast Asian Chinese communities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Southeast Asian Chinese communities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Chinese+characters%22">"Chinese characters"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Chinese+characters%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Chinese+characters%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Chinese+characters%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Chinese+characters%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Chinese+characters%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2012</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> underwent three successive rounds of character simplification. These resulted in some simplifications that differed from those used in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>. It ultimately adopted the reforms of the People's Republic of China in their entirety as official, and has implemented them in the <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Singapore" title="Education in Singapore">educational system</a>. However, unlike in China, personal names may still be registered in traditional characters.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> started teaching a set of simplified characters at schools in 1981, which were also completely identical to the Mainland China simplifications. Chinese newspapers in Malaysia are published in either set of characters, typically with the headlines in traditional Chinese while the body is in simplified Chinese.
</p><p>Although in both countries the use of simplified characters is universal among the younger Chinese generation, a large majority of the older Chinese literate generation still use the traditional characters. Chinese shop signs are also generally written in traditional characters.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, most Chinese schools and businesses still use the traditional characters and <a href="/wiki/Bopomofo" title="Bopomofo">bopomofo</a>, owing from influence from the Republic of China (Taiwan) due to the shared <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> heritage. Recently, however, more Chinese schools now use both simplified characters and <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>. Since most readers of Chinese newspapers in the Philippines belong to the older generation, they are still published largely using traditional characters.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Public and private Chinese signage in the United States and Canada most often use traditional characters.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> There is some effort to get municipal governments to implement more simplified character signage due to recent immigration from mainland China.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Most community newspapers printed in North America are also printed in traditional characters.
</p>
<h3><span id="Comparisons_of_traditional_Chinese.2C_simplified_Chinese.2C_and_Japanese"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparisons_of_traditional_Chinese,_simplified_Chinese,_and_Japanese">Comparisons of traditional Chinese, simplified Chinese, and Japanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Comparisons of traditional Chinese, simplified Chinese, and Japanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following is a comparison of Chinese characters in the <i><a href="/wiki/Standard_Form_of_National_Characters" title="Standard Form of National Characters">Standard Form of National Characters</a></i>, a common <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional Chinese">traditional Chinese</a> standard used in Taiwan, the <i><a href="/wiki/Table_of_General_Standard_Chinese_Characters" title="Table of General Standard Chinese Characters">Table of General Standard Chinese Characters</a></i>, the standard for Mainland Chinese <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified Chinese"><i>jiantizi</i></a> (simplified), and the <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">jōyō kanji</a></i>, the standard for Japanese <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a>. Generally, the <i>jōyō kanji</i> are more similar to <i>fantizi</i> (traditional) than <i>jiantizi</i>  are to <i>fantizi</i>. "Simplified" refers to having significant differences from the Taiwan standard, not necessarily being a newly created character or a newly performed substitution. The characters in the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Forms_of_Frequently_Used_Characters" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Forms of Frequently Used Characters">Hong Kong standard</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Dictionary" title="Kangxi Dictionary">Kangxi Dictionary</a> are also known as "Traditional," but are not shown.
</p>
