<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Written_styles">Written styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Written styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_script_styles" title="Chinese script styles">Chinese script styles</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Treatise_On_Calligraphy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Treatise_On_Calligraphy.jpg/220px-Treatise_On_Calligraphy.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="364" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Treatise_On_Calligraphy.jpg/330px-Treatise_On_Calligraphy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Treatise_On_Calligraphy.jpg/440px-Treatise_On_Calligraphy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="602" data-file-height="997" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Treatise_On_Calligraphy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sample of the cursive script by Chinese <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> calligrapher <a href="/wiki/Sun_Guoting" title="Sun Guoting">Sun Guoting</a>, c. 650&#160;AD</div></div></div>
<p>There are numerous styles, or scripts, in which Chinese characters can be written, deriving from various calligraphic and historical models. Most of these originated in China and are now common, with minor variations, in all countries where Chinese characters are used.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">oracle bone script</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> scripts found on <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">Chinese bronze inscriptions</a> are no longer used; the oldest script that is still in use today is the <a href="/wiki/Seal_Script" class="mw-redirect" title="Seal Script">Seal Script</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">篆書</span>(<span lang="zh-Hans">篆书</span>), <i>zhuànshū</i>). It evolved organically out of the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a> Zhou script, and was adopted in a standardized form under the first <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Emperor of China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a>. The seal script, as the name suggests, is now used only in artistic seals. Few people are still able to read it effortlessly today, although the art of carving a traditional seal in the script remains alive; some <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">calligraphers</a> also work in this style.
</p><p>Scripts that are still used regularly are the "<a href="/wiki/Clerical_Script" class="mw-redirect" title="Clerical Script">Clerical Script</a>" (<span lang="zh-Hant">隸書</span>(<span lang="zh-Hans">隶书</span>), <i>lìshū</i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Wei_Monumental_Script&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wei Monumental Script (page does not exist)">Weibei</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">魏碑</span>, <i>wèibēi</i>), the "<a href="/wiki/Regular_Script" class="mw-redirect" title="Regular Script">Regular Script</a>" (<span lang="zh-Hant">楷書</span>(<span lang="zh-Hans">楷书</span>), <i>kǎishū</i>), which is used mostly for printing, and the "<a href="/wiki/Semi-cursive_Script" class="mw-redirect" title="Semi-cursive Script">Semi-cursive Script</a>" (<span lang="zh-Hant">行書</span>(<span lang="zh-Hans">行书</span>), <i>xíngshū</i>), used mostly for handwriting.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cursive_script_(East_Asia)" title="Cursive script (East Asia)">cursive script</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">草書</span>(<span lang="zh-Hans">草书</span>), <i>cǎoshū</i>, literally "grass script") is used informally. The basic character shapes are suggested, rather than explicitly realized, and the abbreviations are sometimes extreme. Despite being cursive to the point where individual strokes are no longer differentiable and the characters often illegible to the untrained eye, this script (also known as <i>draft</i>) is highly revered for the beauty and freedom that it embodies. Some of the <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese characters</a> adopted by the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>, and some simplified characters used in Japan, are derived from the cursive script. The Japanese <i><a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a></i> script is also derived from this script.
</p><p>There also exist scripts created outside China, such as the Japanese <i><a href="/wiki/Edomoji" title="Edomoji">Edomoji</a></i> styles; these have tended to remain restricted to their countries of origin, rather than spreading to other countries like the Chinese scripts.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calligraphy">Calligraphy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Calligraphy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">Chinese calligraphy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:189px;"><a href="/wiki/File:This_Letter_written_by_Mi_Fei.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/This_Letter_written_by_Mi_Fei.jpg" decoding="async" width="187" height="240" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="187" data-file-height="240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:This_Letter_written_by_Mi_Fei.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese calligraphy of mixed styles written by <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (1051–1108&#160;AD) poet <a href="/wiki/Mi_Fu" title="Mi Fu">Mifu</a>. For centuries, the Chinese literati were expected to master the art of calligraphy.</div></div></div>
<p>The art of writing Chinese characters is called <b>Chinese calligraphy</b>. It is usually done with <a href="/wiki/Ink_brushes" class="mw-redirect" title="Ink brushes">ink brushes</a>. In ancient China, Chinese calligraphy is one of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Arts_of_the_Chinese_Scholars" class="mw-redirect" title="Four Arts of the Chinese Scholars">Four Arts of the Chinese Scholars</a>. There is a minimalist set of rules of Chinese calligraphy. Every character from the Chinese scripts is built into a uniform shape by means of assigning it a geometric area in which the character must occur. Each character has a set number of brushstrokes; none must be added or taken away from the character to enhance it visually, lest the meaning be lost. Finally, strict regularity is not required, meaning the strokes may be accentuated for dramatic effect of individual style. Calligraphy was the means by which scholars could mark their thoughts and teachings for immortality, and as such, represent some of the most precious treasures that can be found from ancient China.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Typography_and_design">Typography and design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Typography and design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%E9%BD%8A%E6%9B%B811.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/%E9%BD%8A%E6%9B%B811.jpg/220px-%E9%BD%8A%E6%9B%B811.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="307" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/%E9%BD%8A%E6%9B%B811.jpg/330px-%E9%BD%8A%E6%9B%B811.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/%E9%BD%8A%E6%9B%B811.jpg/440px-%E9%BD%8A%E6%9B%B811.jpg 2x" data-file-width="793" data-file-height="1107" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%E9%BD%8A%E6%9B%B811.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from a <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> edition of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Qi" title="Book of Qi">Book of Qi</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chenzihmyon_typefaces.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Chenzihmyon_typefaces.svg/220px-Chenzihmyon_typefaces.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Chenzihmyon_typefaces.svg/330px-Chenzihmyon_typefaces.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Chenzihmyon_typefaces.svg/440px-Chenzihmyon_typefaces.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chenzihmyon_typefaces.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first four characters of <i><a href="/wiki/Thousand_Character_Classic" title="Thousand Character Classic">Thousand Character Classic</a></i> in different type and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_script_styles" title="Chinese script styles">script</a> styles. From right to left: <a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">seal script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">regular script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ming_(typeface)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ming (typeface)">Ming</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_sans-serif_typeface" class="mw-redirect" title="East Asian sans-serif typeface">sans-serif</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg/220px-%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg/330px-%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="425" data-file-height="636" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from a <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> publication in a <a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">regular script</a> typeface which resembles the handwriting of <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xun" title="Ouyang Xun">Ouyang Xun</a></div></div></div>
<p>There are three major families of typefaces used in Chinese typography:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ming_(typeface)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ming (typeface)">Song/Ming</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/East_Asian_sans-serif_typeface" class="mw-redirect" title="East Asian sans-serif typeface">Sans-serif</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">Regular script</a></li></ul>
<p>Ming and sans-serif are the most popular in <a href="/wiki/Body_text" title="Body text">body text</a> and are based on regular script for Chinese characters akin to Western <a href="/wiki/Serif" title="Serif">serif</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sans-serif" title="Sans-serif">sans-serif</a> typefaces, respectively. Regular script typefaces emulate <a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">regular script</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ming_(typeface)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ming (typeface)"><i>Song</i> typeface</a> (<span lang="zh-Hans">宋体</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant">宋體</span>, <i>sòngtǐ</i>) is known as the <i>Ming</i> typeface (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">明朝</span></span>, <i>minchō</i>) in Japan, and it is also somewhat more commonly known as the <i>Ming</i> typeface (<span lang="zh-Hans">明体</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant">明體</span>, <i>míngtǐ</i>) than the <i>Song</i> typeface in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>. The names of these styles come from the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming</a> dynasties, when <a href="/wiki/Block_printing" class="mw-redirect" title="Block printing">block printing</a> flourished in China.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/East_Asian_sans-serif_typeface" class="mw-redirect" title="East Asian sans-serif typeface">Sans-serif</a> typefaces, called black typeface (<span lang="zh-Hans">黑体</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant">黑體</span>, <i>hēitǐ</i>) in Chinese and Gothic typeface (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">ゴシック体</span></span>) in Japanese, are characterized by simple lines of even thickness for each stroke, akin to sans-serif styles such as <a href="/wiki/Arial" title="Arial">Arial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helvetica" title="Helvetica">Helvetica</a> in Western typography.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">Regular script</a> typefaces are also commonly used, but not as common as Ming or sans-serif typefaces for body text. Regular script typefaces are often used to teach students Chinese characters, and often aim to match the standard forms of the region where they are meant to be used. Most typefaces in the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> were regular script typefaces which resembled a particular person's handwriting (e.g. the handwriting of <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xun" title="Ouyang Xun">Ouyang Xun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yan_Zhenqing" title="Yan Zhenqing">Yan Zhenqing</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Liu_Gongquan" title="Liu Gongquan">Liu Gongquan</a>), while most modern regular script typefaces tend toward anonymity and regularity.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Variant_Chinese_character" title="Variant Chinese character">Variant Chinese character</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Variations_of_the_213th_Chinese_radical,_gui_%27tortoise%27.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e6/Variations_of_the_213th_Chinese_radical%2C_gui_%27tortoise%27.png/220px-Variations_of_the_213th_Chinese_radical%2C_gui_%27tortoise%27.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="40" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e6/Variations_of_the_213th_Chinese_radical%2C_gui_%27tortoise%27.png/330px-Variations_of_the_213th_Chinese_radical%2C_gui_%27tortoise%27.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e6/Variations_of_the_213th_Chinese_radical%2C_gui_%27tortoise%27.png/440px-Variations_of_the_213th_Chinese_radical%2C_gui_%27tortoise%27.png 2x" data-file-width="1047" data-file-height="192" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Variations_of_the_213th_Chinese_radical,_gui_%27tortoise%27.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Variants of the Chinese character for <i>guī</i> 'turtle', collected c. 1800 from printed sources. The one at left is the traditional form used today in Taiwan and Hong Kong, <span lang="zh-Hant">龜</span>, though <span lang="zh-Hant">龜</span> may look slightly different, or even like the second variant from the left, depending on your font (see <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%BE%9C" class="extiw" title="wikt:龜">Wiktionary</a>). The modern simplified forms used in China, <span lang="zh-Hans">龟</span>, and in Japan, <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">亀</span></span>, are most similar to the variant in the middle of the bottom row, though neither is identical. A few more closely resemble the modern simplified form of the character for <i>diàn</i> 'lightning', <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">电</span>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Four_variant_Chinese_characters.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Four_variant_Chinese_characters.png/220px-Four_variant_Chinese_characters.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="50" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fa/Four_variant_Chinese_characters.png/330px-Four_variant_Chinese_characters.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/fa/Four_variant_Chinese_characters.png 2x" data-file-width="417" data-file-height="95" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Four_variant_Chinese_characters.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Five of the 30 variant characters found in the preface of the <i>Imperial (Kangxi) Dictionary</i> which are not found in the dictionary itself. They are <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">為</span> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">爲</span>) <i>wèi</i> "due to", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">此</span> <i>cǐ</i> "this", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">所</span> <i>suǒ</i> "place", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">能</span> <i>néng</i> "be able to", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">兼</span> <i>jiān</i> "concurrently". (Although the form of <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">為</span> is not very different, and in fact is used today in Japan, the radical <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">爪</span> has been obliterated.) Another variant from the preface, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">来</span> for <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">來</span> <i>lái</i> "to come", also not listed in the dictionary, has been adopted as the standard in Mainland China and Japan.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CJK_variant_glyphs.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/CJK_variant_glyphs.png/220px-CJK_variant_glyphs.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="63" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/CJK_variant_glyphs.png/330px-CJK_variant_glyphs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/CJK_variant_glyphs.png 2x" data-file-width="402" data-file-height="115" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CJK_variant_glyphs.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The character <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">次</span> in <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified Chinese character">Simplified</a> and <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional Chinese character">Traditional</a> Chinese, Japanese, and Korean. If you have an appropriate font installed, you can see the corresponding character in Vietnamese: <big><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r867716940">.mw-parser-output .vi-nom{font-family:"Han-Nom Gothic","Nom Na Tong","Han-Nom Ming","Han-Nom Minh","HAN NOM A","HAN NOM B","Han-Nom Kai","Sun-ExtA","Sun-ExtB","Ming-Lt-HKSCS-UNI-H","Ming-Lt-HKSCS-ExtB","HanaMinA","HanaMinB","HanaMin","MingLiU","MingLiU-ExtB","MingLiU HKSCS","MingLiU HKSCS-ExtB","SimSun","SimSun-ExtB","FZKaiT-Extended","FZKaiT-Extended(SIP)",sans-serif}.mw-parser-output .vi-nom .ext{font-family:"Han-Nom Gothic","Han-Nom Ming","Han-Nom Minh","BabelStone Han","Sun-ExtB","MingLiU HKSCS-ExtB","Ming-Lt-HKSCS-ExtB","HanaMinB","Han-Nom Kai",sans-serif}</style><span lang="vi-Hani" title="Vietnamese language text"><span class="vi-nom">次</span></span></big>.</div></div></div>
<p>Just as <a href="/wiki/Roman_letter" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman letter">Roman letters</a> have a characteristic shape (lower-case letters mostly occupying the <a href="/wiki/X-height" title="X-height">x-height</a>, with ascenders or descenders on some letters), Chinese characters occupy a more or less square area in which the components of every character are written to fit in order to maintain a uniform size and shape, especially with small printed characters in <a href="/wiki/Ming_(typeface)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ming (typeface)">Ming</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_sans-serif_typeface" class="mw-redirect" title="East Asian sans-serif typeface">sans-serif</a> styles. Because of this, beginners often practise writing on squared graph paper, and the Chinese sometimes use the term "Square-Block Characters" (<span lang="zh-Hans">方块字</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant">方塊字</span>, <i>fāngkuàizì</i>), sometimes translated as <i>tetragraph</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> in reference to Chinese characters.
</p><p>Despite standardization, some nonstandard forms are commonly used, especially in handwriting. In older sources, even authoritative ones, variant characters are commonplace. For example, in the preface to the <i><a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Dictionary" title="Kangxi Dictionary">Imperial Dictionary</a></i>, there are 30 variant characters which are not found in the dictionary itself.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> A few of these are reproduced at right.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_standards">Regional standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Regional standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The nature of Chinese characters makes it very easy to produce <a href="/wiki/Allograph" class="mw-redirect" title="Allograph">allographs</a> (variants) for many characters, and there have been many efforts at orthographical standardization throughout history. In recent times, the widespread usage of the characters in several nations has prevented any particular system becoming universally adopted and the standard form of many Chinese characters thus varies in different regions.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">Mainland China</a> adopted <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese characters</a> in 1956. They are also used in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Traditional Chinese characters</a> are used in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. Postwar Japan has used its own less drastically simplified characters, <a href="/wiki/Shinjitai" title="Shinjitai">Shinjitai</a>, since 1946, while <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> has limited its use of Chinese characters, and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> have completely abolished their use in favour of <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_alphabet" title="Vietnamese alphabet">Vietnamese alphabet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a>, respectively.
</p><p>The standard character forms of each region are described in:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Frequently_Used_Characters_in_Modern_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Frequently Used Characters in Modern Chinese">List of Frequently Used Characters in Modern Chinese</a> for Mainland China.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Forms_of_Frequently_Used_Characters" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Forms of Frequently Used Characters">List of Forms of Frequently Used Characters</a> for Hong Kong.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Standard_Form_of_National_Characters" title="Standard Form of National Characters">Standard Form of National Characters</a> for Taiwan.</li>
<li>The list of <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">Jōyō kanji</a> for Japan.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Han-Han_Dae_Sajeon" title="Han-Han Dae Sajeon">Han-Han Dae Sajeon</a> (<i>de facto</i>) for Korea.</li></ul>
<p>In addition to strictness in character size and shape, Chinese characters are written with very precise rules. The most important rules regard the strokes employed, stroke placement, and <a href="/wiki/Stroke_order" title="Stroke order">stroke order</a>. Just as each region that uses Chinese characters has standardized character forms, each also has standardized stroke orders, with each standard being different. Most characters can be written with just one correct stroke order, though some words also have many valid stroke orders, which may occasionally result in different stroke counts. Some characters are also written with different stroke orders due to character simplification.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polysyllabic_morphemes">Polysyllabic morphemes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Polysyllabic morphemes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chinese characters are primarily <a href="/wiki/Morphosyllabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Morphosyllabic">morphosyllabic</a>, meaning that most Chinese <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a> are monosyllabic and are written with a single character, though in modern Chinese most <i>words</i> are disyllabic and dimorphemic, consisting of two syllables, each of which is a morpheme. In modern Chinese 10% of morphemes only occur as part of a given compound. However, a few morphemes are disyllabic, some of them dating back to Classical Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman19888–9_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman19888–9-102">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Excluding foreign loan words, these are typically words for plants and small animals. They are usually written with a pair of phono-semantic compound characters sharing a common radical. Examples are <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">蝴蝶</span> <i>húdié</i> "butterfly" and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">珊瑚</span> <i>shānhú</i> "coral". Note that the <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">蝴</span> <i>hú</i> of <i>húdié</i> and the <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">瑚</span> <i>hú</i> of <i>shānhú</i> have the same phonetic, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">胡</span>, but different radicals ("insect" and "jade", respectively). Neither exists as an independent morpheme except as a poetic abbreviation of the disyllabic word.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polysyllabic_characters">Polysyllabic characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Polysyllabic characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In certain cases compound words and set phrases may be contracted into single characters. Some of these can be considered <a href="/wiki/Logogram" title="Logogram">logograms</a>, where characters represent whole words rather than syllable-morphemes, though these are generally instead considered <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ligature" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese ligature">ligatures</a> or abbreviations (similar to <a href="/wiki/Scribal_abbreviations" class="mw-redirect" title="Scribal abbreviations">scribal abbreviations</a>, such as &amp; for "et"), and as non-standard. These do see use, particularly in handwriting or decoration, but also in some cases in print. In Chinese, these ligatures are called <i>héwén</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%90%88%E6%96%87" class="extiw" title="zh:合文">合文</a></span>), <i>héshū</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">合書</span>) or hétǐzì (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">合体字</span>), and in the special case of combining two characters, these are known as "two-syllable Chinese characters" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">双音节汉字</span>, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">雙音節漢字</span>).
</p><p>A commonly seen example is the <a href="/wiki/Double_Happiness_(calligraphy)" title="Double Happiness (calligraphy)">double happiness</a> symbol <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">囍</span>, formed as a ligature of <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">喜喜</span> and referred to by its disyllabic name (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hans">双喜</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hant">雙喜</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shuāngxǐ</span></i>). In handwriting, numbers are very frequently squeezed into one space or combined – common ligatures include <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">廿</span> <i>niàn,</i> "twenty", normally read as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">二十</span> <i>èrshí,</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">卅</span> <i>sà,</i> "thirty", normally read as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">三十</span> <i>sānshí,</i> and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">卌</span> xì "forty", normally read as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">四十</span> "sìshí". Calendars often use numeral ligatures in order to save space;  for example, the "21st of March" can be read as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">三月廿一</span><i>.</i> In some cases counters are also merged into one character, such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">七十人</span> <i>qīshí rén</i> "seventy people". Another common abbreviation is <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">门</span> with a "T" written inside it, for <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">問題</span>, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">问题</span>, <i>wèntí</i> ("question; problem"), where the "T" is from pinyin for the second syllable <i>tí</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">题</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-vmair_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vmair-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Since polysyllabic characters are often non-standard, they are often excluded in character dictionaries.
</p><p>Modern examples particularly include <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units#Chinese_and_Japanese" title="International System of Units">Chinese characters</a> for <a href="/wiki/SI_units" class="mw-redirect" title="SI units">SI units</a>. In Chinese these units are disyllabic and standardly written with two characters, as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">厘米</span> <i>límǐ</i> "centimeter" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">厘</span> centi-, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">米</span> meter) or <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">千瓦</span> <i>qiānwǎ</i> "kilowatt". However, in the 19th century these were often written via compound characters, pronounced disyllabically, such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">瓩</span> for <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">千瓦</span> or <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">糎</span> for <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">厘米</span> – some of these characters were also used in Japan, where they were pronounced with borrowed European readings instead. These have now fallen out of general use, but are occasionally seen. Less systematic examples include <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">圕</span> <i>túshūguǎn</i> "library", a contraction of <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">圖書館</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of such contractions is as old as Chinese characters themselves, and they have frequently been found in religious or ritual use. In the Oracle Bone script, personal names, ritual items, and even phrases such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">受又</span>(<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">祐</span>) <i>shòu yòu</i> "receive blessings" are commonly contracted into single characters. A dramatic example is that in medieval manuscripts <span lang="zh-Hant">菩薩</span> <i>púsà</i> "bodhisattva" (simplified: <span lang="zh-Hans">菩萨</span>) is sometimes written with a single character formed of a 2×2 grid of four <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">十</span> (derived from the grass radical over two <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">十</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-vmair_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vmair-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> However, for the sake of consistency and standardization, the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">CPC</a> seeks to limit the use of such polysyllabic characters in public writing to ensure that every character only has one syllable.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Conversely, with the fusion of the diminutive <i>-er</i> suffix in Mandarin, some monosyllabic words may even be written with two characters, as in <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">花儿</span> <i>huār</i> "flower", which was formerly disyllabic.
</p><p>In most other languages that use the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_family_of_scripts" title="Chinese family of scripts">Chinese family of scripts</a>, notably Korean, Vietnamese, and Zhuang, Chinese characters are typically monosyllabic, but in Japanese a single character is generally used to represent a borrowed monosyllabic Chinese morpheme (the <i><a href="/wiki/On%27yomi" class="mw-redirect" title="On&#39;yomi">on'yomi</a></i>), a polysyllabic native Japanese morpheme (the <i><a href="/wiki/Kun%27yomi" class="mw-redirect" title="Kun&#39;yomi">kun'yomi</a></i>), or even (in rare cases) a foreign loanword. These uses are completely standard and unexceptional.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rare_and_complex_characters">Rare and complex characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Rare and complex characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Often a character not commonly used (a "rare" or "variant" character) will appear in a personal or place name in Chinese, Japanese, Korean, and Vietnamese (see <a href="/wiki/Chinese_name" title="Chinese name">Chinese name</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_name" title="Japanese name">Japanese name</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_name" title="Korean name">Korean name</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_name" title="Vietnamese name">Vietnamese name</a>, respectively). This has caused problems as many computer encoding systems include only the most common characters and exclude the less often used characters. This is especially a problem for personal names which often contain rare or classical, antiquated characters.
</p><p>One man who has encountered this problem is Taiwanese politician <a href="/wiki/Yu_Shyi-kun" title="Yu Shyi-kun">Yu Shyi-kun</a>, due to the rarity of the last character in his name. Newspapers have dealt with this problem in varying ways, including using software to combine two existing, similar characters, including a picture of the personality, or, especially as is the case with Yu Shyi-kun, simply substituting a homophone for the rare character in the hope that the reader would be able to make the correct inference. Taiwanese political posters, movie posters etc. will often add the <a href="/wiki/Bopomofo" title="Bopomofo">bopomofo</a> phonetic symbols next to such a character. Japanese newspapers may render such names and words in <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> instead, and it is accepted practice for people to write names for which they are unsure of the correct kanji in katakana instead.
</p><p>There are also some extremely complex characters which have understandably become rather rare. According to <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%ABl_Bellassen" title="Joël Bellassen">Joël Bellassen</a> (1989), the most complex Chinese character is <a href="/wiki/File:Zh%C3%A9.svg" class="image"><img alt="Zhé.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Zh%C3%A9.svg/25px-Zh%C3%A9.svg.png" decoding="async" width="25" height="25" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Zh%C3%A9.svg/38px-Zh%C3%A9.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Zh%C3%A9.svg/50px-Zh%C3%A9.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></a>/<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%AA%9A%A5" class="extiw" title="wikt:𪚥">𪚥</a></span> (U+2A6A5) <i>zhé</i> <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zh-zhe2.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/41/Zh-zhe2.ogg" class="internal" title="Zh-zhe2.ogg">listen</a></span>&#160;<small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Zh-zhe2.ogg" title="File:Zh-zhe2.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>, meaning "verbose" and containing sixty-four strokes; this character fell from use around the 5th century. It might be argued, however, that while containing the most strokes, it is not necessarily the most complex character (in terms of difficulty), as it simply requires writing the same sixteen-stroke character <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">龍</span> <i>lóng</i> (lit. "dragon") four times in the space for one. Another 64-stroke character is <a href="/wiki/File:Zh%C3%A8ng.svg" class="image"><img alt="Zhèng.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Zh%C3%A8ng.svg/25px-Zh%C3%A8ng.svg.png" decoding="async" width="25" height="25" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Zh%C3%A8ng.svg/38px-Zh%C3%A8ng.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Zh%C3%A8ng.svg/50px-Zh%C3%A8ng.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></a>/<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%A0%94%BB" class="extiw" title="wikt:𠔻">𠔻</a></span> (U+2053B) <i>zhèng</i> composed of <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">興</span> <i>xīng/xìng</i> (lit. "flourish") four times.
</p><p>One of the most complex characters found in modern Chinese dictionaries<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;g&#93;</a></sup> is <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%BD%89" class="extiw" title="wikt:齉">齉</a></span> (U+9F49) (<i>nàng</i>, <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zh-nang4.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/26/Zh-nang4.ogg" class="internal" title="Zh-nang4.ogg">listen</a></span>&#160;<small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Zh-nang4.ogg" title="File:Zh-nang4.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>, pictured below, middle image), meaning "snuffle" (that is, a pronunciation marred by a blocked nose), with "just" thirty-six strokes. Another stroke-rich character is 靐 (<i>bìng</i>), with 39 strokes, meaning the loud noise thunder. However, these are not in common use. The most complex character that can be input using the Microsoft New Phonetic IME 2002a for traditional Chinese is <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%BE%98" class="extiw" title="wikt:龘">龘</a></span> (<i>dá</i>, "the appearance of a dragon flying"). It is composed of the dragon radical represented three times, for a total of 16 × 3 = 48 strokes. Among the most complex characters in modern dictionaries and also in <i>frequent modern use</i> are <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%B1%B2" class="extiw" title="wikt:籲">籲</a></span> (<i>yù</i>, "to implore"), with 32 strokes; <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%AC%B1" class="extiw" title="wikt:鬱">鬱</a></span> (<i>yù</i>, "luxuriant, lush; gloomy"), with 29 strokes, as in <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">憂鬱</span> (<i>yōuyù</i>, "depressed"); <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%B1%94" class="extiw" title="wikt:豔">豔</a></span> (<i>yàn</i>, "colorful"), with 28 strokes; and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%87%81" class="extiw" title="wikt:釁">釁</a></span> (<i>xìn</i>, "quarrel"), with 25 strokes, as in <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">挑釁</span> (<i>tiǎoxìn</i>, "to pick a fight"). Also in occasional modern use is <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%B1%BB" class="extiw" title="wikt:鱻">鱻</a></span> (<i>xiān</i> "fresh"; variant of <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">鮮</span> <i>xiān</i>) with 33 strokes.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Japanese_writing_system" title="Japanese writing system">Japanese</a>, an 84-stroke <i><a href="/wiki/Kokuji" class="mw-redirect" title="Kokuji">kokuji</a></i> exists: <a href="/wiki/File:Taito_1.svg" class="image"><img alt="Taito 1.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Taito_1.svg/25px-Taito_1.svg.png" decoding="async" width="25" height="25" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Taito_1.svg/38px-Taito_1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Taito_1.svg/50px-Taito_1.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></a>, normally read <i><a href="/wiki/Taito_(kanji)" title="Taito (kanji)">taito</a></i>. It is composed of three "cloud" (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">雲</span></span>) characters on top of the abovementioned triple "dragon" character (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">龘</span></span>). Also meaning "the appearance of a dragon in flight", it has been pronounced <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">おとど</span></span> <i>otodo</i>, <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text"><a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E3%81%9F%E3%81%84%E3%81%A8" class="extiw" title="ja:たいと">たいと</a></span></span> <i>taito</i>, and <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">だいと</span></span> <i>daito</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The most elaborate character in the <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji_list" class="mw-redirect" title="Jōyō kanji list">jōyō kanji list</a> is the 29-stroke <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">鬱</span></span>, meaning "depression" or "melancholy".
</p><p>The most complex Chinese character still in use may be<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (March 2012)">according to whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <i>biáng</i> (pictured right, bottom), with 58 strokes, which refers to <a href="/wiki/Biang_biang_noodles" class="mw-redirect" title="Biang biang noodles">Biang biang noodles</a>, a type of noodle from <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a> province. This character along with the syllable <i>biáng</i> cannot be found in dictionaries. The fact that it represents a syllable that does not exist in any <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> word means that it could be classified as a dialectal character.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_of_characters">Number of characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Number of characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The total number of Chinese characters from past to present remains unknowable because new ones are being developed all the time – for instance, brands may create new characters when none of the existing ones allow for the intended meaning – or they have been invented by whoever wrote them and have never been adopted as official characters. Chinese characters are theoretically an open set and anyone can create new characters, though such inventions are rarely included in official character sets.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The number of entries in major Chinese dictionaries is the best means of estimating the historical growth of character inventory.
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Even the <i>Zhonghua Zihai</i> does not include characters in the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_family_of_scripts" title="Chinese family of scripts">Chinese family of scripts</a> created to represent non-Chinese languages, except the unique characters in use in Japan and Korea. Characters formed by Chinese principles in other languages include the roughly 1,500 Japanese-made <i><a href="/wiki/Kokuji" class="mw-redirect" title="Kokuji">kokuji</a></i> given in the <i><a href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E5%9B%BD%E5%AD%97%E3%81%AE%E5%AD%97%E5%85%B8" class="extiw" title="ja:国字の字典">Kokuji no Jiten</a>,</i><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> the Korean-made <a href="/wiki/Gukja" class="mw-redirect" title="Gukja">gukja</a>, the over 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Sawndip" title="Sawndip">Sawndip</a> characters still in use in <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>, and the almost 20,000 <a href="/wiki/N%C3%B4m" class="mw-redirect" title="Nôm">Nôm</a> characters formerly used in Vietnam.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> More divergent descendants of Chinese script include <a href="/wiki/Tangut_script" title="Tangut script">Tangut script</a>, which created over 5,000 characters with similar strokes but different formation principles to Chinese characters.
</p><p>Modified radicals and new variants are two common reasons for the ever-increasing number of characters. There are about 300 radicals and 100 are in common use. Creating a new character by modifying the radical is an easy way to disambiguate <a href="/wiki/Homograph" title="Homograph">homographs</a> among <i>xíngshēngzì</i> pictophonetic compounds. This practice began long before the standardization of Chinese script by <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a> and continues to the present day. The traditional 3rd-person pronoun <i>tā</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">他</span> "he, she, it"), which is written with the "person radical", illustrates modifying significs to form new characters. In modern usage, there is a graphic distinction between <i>tā</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">她</span> "she") with the "woman radical", <i>tā</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">牠</span> "it") with the "animal radical", <i>tā</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">它</span> "it") with the "roof radical", and <i>tā</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">祂</span> "He") with the "deity radical", One consequence of modifying radicals is the fossilization of rare and obscure variant logographs, some of which are not even used in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a>. For instance, <i>he</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">和</span>  "harmony, peace", which combines the "grain radical" with the "mouth radical", has infrequent variants <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">咊</span> with the radicals reversed and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">龢</span> with the "flute radical".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese">Chinese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Chinese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cumulative_Hanzi_frequency.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cumulative_Hanzi_frequency.svg/220px-Cumulative_Hanzi_frequency.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cumulative_Hanzi_frequency.svg/330px-Cumulative_Hanzi_frequency.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cumulative_Hanzi_frequency.svg/440px-Cumulative_Hanzi_frequency.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cumulative_Hanzi_frequency.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cumulative frequency of simplified Chinese characters in Modern Chinese text<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Chinese characters should not be confused with Chinese words, as the majority of modern Chinese words, unlike their <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> counterparts, are written with two or more characters, each character representing one syllable and/or <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a>. Knowing the meanings of the individual characters of a word will often allow the general meaning of the word to be inferred, but this is not always the case.
</p><p>Studies in China have shown that literate individuals know and use between 3,000 and 4,000 characters.  Specialists in classical literature or history, who would often encounter characters no longer in use, are estimated to have a working vocabulary of between 5,000 and 6,000 characters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198873_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198873-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, which uses <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese characters</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Xi%C3%A0nd%C3%A0i_H%C3%A0ny%C7%94_Ch%C3%A1ngy%C3%B2ng_Z%C3%ACbi%C7%8Eo" class="mw-redirect" title="Xiàndài Hànyǔ Chángyòng Zìbiǎo">Xiàndài Hànyǔ Chángyòng Zìbiǎo</a></i> (<span lang="zh-Hans-CN" title="Chinese language text">现代汉语常用字表</span>, Chart of Common Characters of Modern Chinese) lists 2,500 common characters and 1,000 less-than-common characters, while the <i>Xiàndài Hànyǔ Tōngyòng Zìbiǎo</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans-CN" title="Chinese language text">现代汉语通用字表</span>, Chart of Generally Utilized Characters of Modern Chinese) lists 7,000 characters, including the 3,500 characters already listed above. <a href="/wiki/GB2312" class="mw-redirect" title="GB2312">GB2312</a>, an early version of the national encoding standard used in the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>, has 6,763 code points. <a href="/wiki/GB18030" class="mw-redirect" title="GB18030">GB18030</a>, the modern, mandatory standard, has a much higher number. The New <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A0ny%C7%94_Shu%C7%90p%C3%ADng_K%C7%8Eosh%C3%AC" class="mw-redirect" title="Hànyǔ Shuǐpíng Kǎoshì">Hànyǔ Shuǐpíng Kǎoshì</a> (<span lang="zh-Hans-CN" title="Chinese language text">汉语水平考试</span>, Chinese Proficiency Test) covers approximately 2,600 characters at its highest level (level six).<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> (Taiwan), which uses <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese characters</a>, the Ministry of Education's <i>Chángyòng Guózì Biāozhǔn Zìtǐ Biǎo</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant-TW" title="Chinese language text">常用國字標準字體表</span>, Chart of Standard Forms of Common National Characters) lists 4,808 characters; the <i>Cì Chángyòng Guózì Biāozhǔn Zìtǐ Biǎo</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant-TW" title="Chinese language text">次常用國字標準字體表</span>, Chart of Standard Forms of Less-Than-Common National Characters) lists another 6,341 characters. The <i>Chinese Standard Interchange Code</i> (<a href="/wiki/CNS11643" class="mw-redirect" title="CNS11643">CNS11643</a>)—the official national encoding standard—supports 48,027 characters, while the most widely used encoding scheme, <a href="/wiki/Big5" title="Big5">BIG-5</a>, supports only 13,053.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, which uses <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese characters</a>, the Education and Manpower Bureau's <i>Soengjung Zi Zijing Biu</i> (<span lang="zh-Hant-HK" title="Chinese language text">常用字字形表</span>), intended for use in elementary and junior secondary education, lists a total of 4,759 characters.
</p><p>In addition, there are a number of <i>dialect characters</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">方言字</span>) that are not generally used in formal written Chinese but represent colloquial terms in nonstandard <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a>.  In general, it is common practice to use standard characters to transcribe Chinese dialects when obvious cognates with words in Standard Mandarin exist.  However, when no obvious cognate could be found for a word, due to factors like irregular sound change or semantic drift in the meanings of characters, or the word originates from a non-Chinese source like a substratum from an earlier displaced language or a later borrowing from another language family, then characters are borrowed and used according to the rebus principle or invented in an <i>ad hoc</i> manner to transcribe it.  These new characters are generally phonosemantic compounds (e.g., 侬, 'person' in Min), although a few are compound ideographs (e.g., 孬, 'bad', in Northeast Mandarin). Except in the case of Written Cantonese, there is no official orthography, and there may be several ways to write a dialectal word, often one that is etymologically correct and one or several that are based on the current pronunciation (e.g., 触祭 (etymological) vs. 戳鸡 (phonetic), 'eat' (low-register) in Shanghainese).  Speakers of a dialect will generally recognize a dialectal word if it is transcribed according to phonetic considerations, while the etymologically correct form may be more difficult or impossible to recognize.  For example, few Gan speakers would recognize the character meaning 'to lean' in their dialect, because this character (隑) has become archaic in Standard Mandarin. The historically "correct" transcription is often so obscure that it is uncovered only after considerable scholarly research into philology and historical phonology and may be disputed by other researchers.
</p><p>As an exception, <a href="/wiki/Written_Cantonese" title="Written Cantonese">Written Cantonese</a> is in widespread use in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, even for certain formal documents, due to the former British <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">colonial</a> administration's recognition of Cantonese for use for official purposes. In Taiwan, there is also a body of semi-official characters used to represent <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese Hokkien</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a>.  For example, the vernacular character <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">㓾</span>, pronounced <i>cii<sup>11</sup></i> in <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a>, means "to kill".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Other varieties of Chinese with a significant number of speakers, like <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a> Wu, <a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">Gan Chinese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sichuanese_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Sichuanese Mandarin">Sichuanese</a>, also have their own series of characters, but these are not often seen, except on advertising billboards directed toward locals and are not used in formal settings except to give precise transcriptions of witness statements in legal proceedings.  Written Standard Mandarin is the preference for all mainland regions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_2">Japanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Japanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Japanese there are 2,136 <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">jōyō kanji</a></i> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">常用漢字</span>, lit. "frequently used Chinese characters") designated by the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Ministry_of_Education" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese Ministry of Education">Japanese Ministry of Education</a>; these are taught during primary and secondary school. The list is a recommendation, not a restriction, and many characters missing from it are still in common use.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One area where character usage is officially restricted is in names, which may contain only government-approved characters. Since the <i>jōyō kanji</i> list excludes many characters that have been used in personal and place names for generations, an additional list, referred to as the  <i><a href="/wiki/Jinmeiy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jinmeiyō kanji">jinmeiyō kanji</a></i> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">人名用漢字</span>, lit. "kanji for use in personal names"), is published.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> It currently contains 983 characters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Today, a well-educated Japanese person may know upwards of 3,500 characters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Kanji_kentei" class="mw-redirect" title="Kanji kentei">kanji kentei</a></i> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">日本漢字能力検定試験</span>, <i>Nihon Kanji Nōryoku Kentei Shiken</i> or <i>Test of Japanese Kanji Aptitude</i>) tests a speaker's ability to read and write kanji. The highest level of the <i>kanji kentei</i> tests on approximately 6,000 kanji,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> though in practice few people attain (or need to attain) this level.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_creation">Modern creation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Modern creation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>New characters can in principle be coined at any time, just as new words can be, but they may not be adopted. Significant historically recent coinages date to scientific terms of the 19th century. Specifically, Chinese coined new characters for chemical elements – see <a href="/wiki/Chemical_elements_in_East_Asian_languages" title="Chemical elements in East Asian languages">chemical elements in East Asian languages</a> – which continue to be used and taught in schools in China and Taiwan. In Japan, in the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Meiji era">Meiji era</a> (specifically, late 19th century), new characters were coined for some (but not all) SI units, such as <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">粁</span></span> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">米</span></span> "meter" + <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">千</span></span> "thousand, kilo-") for kilometer. These <a href="/wiki/Kokuji" class="mw-redirect" title="Kokuji">kokuji</a> (Japanese-coinages) have found use in China as well – see <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units#Chinese_and_Japanese" title="International System of Units">Chinese characters for SI units</a> for details.
</p><p>While new characters can be easily coined by writing on paper, they are difficult to represent on a computer – they must generally be represented as a picture, rather than as text – which presents a significant barrier to their use or widespread adoption. Compare this with the use of symbols as names in 20th century musical albums such as <i><a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_IV" title="Led Zeppelin IV">Led Zeppelin IV</a></i> (1971) and <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Symbol_Album" class="mw-redirect" title="Love Symbol Album">Love Symbol Album</a></i> (1993); an album cover may potentially contain any graphics, but in writing and other computation these symbols are difficult to use.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Indexing">Indexing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Indexing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Dozens of indexing schemes have been created for arranging Chinese characters in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dictionary" title="Chinese dictionary">Chinese dictionaries</a>. The great majority of these schemes have appeared in only a single dictionary; only one such system has achieved truly widespread use. This is the system of <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_character)" class="mw-redirect" title="Radical (Chinese character)">radicals</a> (see for example, the 214 so-called <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_radical" title="Kangxi radical">Kangxi radicals</a>).
</p><p>Chinese character dictionaries often allow users to locate entries in several ways. Many Chinese, Japanese, and Korean dictionaries of Chinese characters list characters in radical order: characters are grouped together by radical, and radicals containing fewer <a href="/wiki/Stroke_(Chinese_character)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stroke (Chinese character)">strokes</a> come before radicals containing more strokes (<a href="/wiki/Radical-and-stroke_sorting" class="mw-redirect" title="Radical-and-stroke sorting">radical-and-stroke sorting</a>). Under each radical, characters are listed by their total number of strokes. It is often also possible to search for characters by sound, using <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a> (in Chinese dictionaries), <a href="/wiki/Bopomofo" title="Bopomofo">zhuyin</a> (in Taiwanese dictionaries), <a href="/wiki/Kana" title="Kana">kana</a> (in Japanese dictionaries) or <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">hangul</a> (in Korean dictionaries). Most dictionaries also allow searches by total number of strokes, and individual dictionaries often allow other search methods as well.
</p><p>For instance, to look up the character where the sound is not known, e.g., <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">松</span> (pine tree), the user first determines which part of the character is the radical (here <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">木</span>), then counts the number of strokes in the radical (four), and turns to the radical index (usually located on the inside front or back cover of the dictionary). Under the number "4" for radical stroke count, the user locates <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">木</span>, then turns to the page number listed, which is the start of the listing of all the characters containing this radical. This page will have a sub-index giving remainder stroke numbers (for the non-radical portions of characters) and page numbers. The right half of the character also contains four strokes, so the user locates the number 4, and turns to the page number given. From there, the user must scan the entries to locate the character he or she is seeking. Some dictionaries have a sub-index which lists every character containing each radical, and if the user knows the number of strokes in the non-radical portion of the character, he or she can locate the correct page directly.
</p><p>Another dictionary system is the <a href="/wiki/Four_corner_method" class="mw-redirect" title="Four corner method">four corner method</a>, where characters are classified according to the shape of each of the four corners.
</p><p>Most modern Chinese dictionaries and Chinese dictionaries sold to English speakers use the traditional radical-based character index in a section at the front, while the main body of the dictionary arranges the main character entries alphabetically according to their <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a> spelling.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> To find a character with unknown sound using one of these dictionaries, the reader finds the radical and stroke number of the character, as before, and locates the character in the radical index. The character's entry will have the character's pronunciation in pinyin written down; the reader then turns to the main dictionary section and looks up the pinyin spelling alphabetically.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Abbreviations are occasionally used – see <a href="#Polysyllabic_characters">§&#160;Polysyllabic characters</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFQiu2000">Qiu 2000</a>, pp.&#160;132–133 provides archaeological evidence for this dating, in contrast to unsubstantiated claims dating the beginning of cursive anywhere from the Qin to the Eastern Han.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFQiu2000">Qiu 2000</a>, pp.&#160;140–141 mentions examples of neo-clerical with "strong overtones of cursive script" from the late Eastern Han.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-71">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Liu is said to have taught <a href="/wiki/Zhong_Yao" title="Zhong Yao">Zhong Yao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wáng Xīzhī is so credited in essays by other calligraphers in the 6th to early 7th centuries, and most of his extant pieces are in modern cursive script.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000148_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000148-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">cf. <a href="/wiki/Inariyama_Sword" title="Inariyama Sword">Inariyama Sword</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-106"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-106">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/File:N%C3%A0ng.svg" class="image"><img alt="Nàng.svg" data-file-width="538" data-file-height="531" /></a> (U+9F49) <i>nàng</i> is found, for instance, on p. 707 of <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">漢英辭典(修訂版)</span> <i>A Chinese–English Dictionary</i>, (Revised Edition) Foreign Language Teaching and Research Press, Beijing, 1995. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r935243608">.mw-parser-output <li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://zhongwen.com/d/164/x93.htm">"也"</a>. <i>中文.com</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">17 February</span> 2015</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=%E4%B8%AD%E6%96%87.com&amp;rft.atitle=%E4%B9%9F&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fzhongwen.com%2Fd%2F164%2Fx93.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChinese+characters" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i><a href="/wiki/Shuowen_Jiezi" title="Shuowen Jiezi">Shuowen Jiezi</a></i> gives the origin of 也 as “女陰也”, or 'female <i>yin</i> [organ]'.  (The 也 in the definition itself is a declarative sentence final particle.)  By the Classical period (6th century&#160;BC), the original definition had fallen into disuse, and all appearances of the character in texts of that period and in later in Literary Chinese use it as a phonetic loan for the grammatical particle.  In addition to serving as a classical particle, it has acquired the modern Vernacular Chinese meaning of 'also'.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"...East Asia had been among the ﬁrst regions of the world to produce written records of the past. Well into modern times Chinese script, the common script across East Asia, served—with local adaptations and variations—as the normative medium of record-keeping and written historical narrative, as well as ofﬁcial communication. This was true, not only in China itself, but in Korea, Japan, and Vietnam." The Oxford History of Historical Writing. Volume 3. Jose Rabasa, Masayuki Sato, Edoardo Tortarolo, Daniel Woolf – 1400–1800, p. 2.</span>

<dl><dt>History and construction of Chinese characters</dt></dl>
<ul><li>Excerpt from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.pinyin.info/readings/texts/visible/index.html">Visible Speech: The Diverse Oneness of Writing Systems</a> by John DeFrancis, © 1989 by the University of Hawai`i Press. Used by permission of the University of Hawai`i Press.</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Online dictionaries and character reference</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ctext.org/dictionary.pl?if=en">Chinese Text Project Dictionary</a> Comprehensive character dictionary including data for all Chinese characters in Unicode, and exemplary usage from early Chinese texts.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.omniglot.com/writing/chinese_evolution.htm">Evolution of Chinese Characters</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://zhongwen.com/">Zhongwen.com</a>: a searchable dictionary with information about character formation</li>
<li>Richard Sears, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100620163409/http://chineseetymology.org/">Chinese Etymology</a>.</li>
<li>Da, Jun, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://lingua.mtsu.edu/chinese-computing/">Chinese text computing</a>&#160;– statistics on use of Chinese characters</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Chinese characters in computing</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.unicode.org/charts/unihan.html">Unihan Database</a>: Chinese, Japanese, and Korean references, readings, and meanings for all the Chinese and Chinese-derived characters in the <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> character set</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cchar.com/">cchar.com Chinese Character Software</a>: Step by step pictures showing how to write Chinese characters.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cymraeg.ru/daoulagad.html">Daoulagad Han</a>&#160;– Mobile OCR hanzi dictionary, OCR interface to the UniHan database</li></ul>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2011-05-15</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Chinese+and+English+dictionary%3A+compiled+from+reliable+authors&amp;rft.pub=American+Tract+Society&amp;rft.date=1893&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DrDQrAAAAYAAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AChinese+characters" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/></li>
